The Syllogism

The Five Types of Qiyas Based on Premise Material
Author classifies qiyas into five types by premise material.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish each of the five types of qiyas by their material and identify the type of a given syllogism from its premises' epistemic status.

Core Idea
The material of the premises determines the syllogism's type and epistemological value.

In the previous lesson, we learned that qiyas (syllogism) consists of premises and a conclusion. But not all syllogisms are equal: their strength depends on the material (maddah) of their premises. In this passage, the author classifies qiyas into five types based on whether the premises are certain, accepted, famous, imaginative, or deceptive. This classification is crucial for evaluating arguments in Islamic sciences, where a demonstrative proof (burhan) is sought in theology and law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five materials
From the Arabic, extract the five adjectives describing the material: yaqiniyya (يقينية), musallama (مسلمة), mashhura (مشهورة), mukhkhayyala (مخيلة), mumawwaha (مموهة). These are the epistemic statuses of the premises.
The terms are in the feminine singular because they modify 'maddah' (material).
2
Match each material to its syllogism type
The author pairs each material with a type: yaqiniyya → burhani, musallama → jadali, mashhura → khatabi, mukhkhayyala → shi'ri, mumawwaha → sufasta'i. Memorize these pairings.
The term 'shi'ri' is specifically linked to imaginative premises, not actual poetry.
3
Understand the epistemological significance
Certain premises yield apodictic knowledge (burhan). Accepted premises are agreed upon in dialectic (jadal). Famous premises are widely held opinions used in rhetoric (khataba). Imaginative premises appeal to emotion (poetry). Deceptive premises are fallacious (sophistry).
Each type has a distinct purpose: demonstration seeks truth, dialectic trains debate, rhetoric persuades, poetry moves, sophistry misleads.
4
Note the overlap and terminological variations
The author states that burhani, jadali, and khatabi can overlap. Also, some scholars call khatabi 'probable' (zanni) or 'persuasive' (iqna'i). This shows that the classification is not rigid.
Overlap occurs because a single syllogism could have premises that are both certain and famous, depending on the audience.
5
Relate to Islamic sciences
In Kalam, theological proofs should be burhani (certain). In Munazara (debate), jadali premises are used to engage opponents. In Khutbah, khatabi arguments persuade the public. Shi'ri syllogisms appear in didactic poetry. Sufasta'i arguments are condemned.
Identifying the type helps evaluate whether an argument is appropriate for its context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ملخص أصول المنطق واصطلاحاته. أقسام. هذه الثلاثة اصطلاح طائفة منهم، كما قد بسطته وذكرتُ أسماءهم في غير هذا الموضع. و«القياس» إن كانت مادته «يقينية» فهو «البرهاني» خاصة، وإن كانت «مسلَّمة» فهو «الجَدَلي»، وإن كانت «مشهورة» فهو «الخطابي»، وإن كانت «مخيَّلة» فهو «الشعري» باعتبار المواد، وإن كانت «مموهة» فهو «السوفسطائي». ولهذا قد يتداخل البرهاني، والخطابي، والجَدَلي؛ وبعض الناس يجعل الخطابي هو «الظني»؛ وبعضهم يجعله «الإقناعي». ولهم اصطلاحات أُخَر، بعضها موافق لاصطلاح «المعلم الأول أرسطو»، وبعضها مخالف له. فإن كثيراً من المصنفين فيه خَرَجوا في كثير منه عن طريقة معلمهم الأول، ولكن ليس المقصود هنا بسط هذا.
Summary of the principles of logic and its terminologies. Sections: These three are the terminology of a group of them, as I have expanded and mentioned their names elsewhere. And the syllogism, if its material is certain, it is specifically the demonstrative; if it is accepted, it is the dialectical; if it is famous, it is the rhetorical; if it is imaginative, it is the poetic in terms of material; and if it is deceptive, it is the sophistical. And because of this, the demonstrative, rhetorical, and dialectical may overlap. Some people make the rhetorical the probable; and some make it the persuasive. And they have other terminologies, some of which agree with the terminology of the First Teacher Aristotle, and some disagree with him. For many authors in this field have departed in many respects from the method of their First Teacher, but the intention here is not to expand on this.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage provides a classification of the syllogism (qiyas) according to the material (maddah) of its premises. The material determines the type of syllogism and its function in argumentation. The author lists five types: demonstrative (burhani) using certain premises (yaqiniyya), dialectical (jadali) using accepted premises (musallama), rhetorical (khatabi) using famous premises (mashhura), poetic (shi'ri) using imaginative premises (mukhkhayyala), and sophistical (sufasta'i) using deceptive premises (mumawwaha). The author notes that these types can overlap, especially demonstrative, rhetorical, and dialectical. There are also terminological variations: some call the rhetorical 'probable' or 'persuasive'. The author indicates that many logicians have departed from Aristotle's original classifications, but he does not elaborate here.

Key Takeaway
The material of a syllogism's premises (certain, accepted, famous, imaginative, or deceptive) defines its type and epistemological weight, a crucial distinction in Islamic argumentation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the following syllogism according to its material: 'Every intoxicating drink is forbidden. This nabidh is intoxicating. Therefore, this nabidh is forbidden.' If the premise 'Every intoxicating drink is forbidden' is taken as certain from the Qur'an and Sunnah, what type is it? If it is accepted by the interlocutor in a debate, what type would it be? Explain the difference.
?

Give one real example of a jadali argument from a known theological debate.

Classification of Qiyas by Form and Matter
Author lists the three forms and five matters of qiyas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a qiyas according to its form (categorical, conditional conjunctive, conditional disjunctive) and its matter (demonstrative, dialectical, rhetorical, poetical, sophistical).

Core Idea
Qiyas is classified by form into three and by matter into five – the skeleton of all logical inference.

In this passage, the author lays out the basic framework for analyzing any syllogism. You will learn that every qiyas has a form (sura) and a matter (madda). The form tells you the logical structure – whether it's categorical or conditional – and the matter tells you the epistemic level of its premises. This distinction is crucial for evaluating arguments in Islamic theology and law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three forms
Find the three forms mentioned: qiyas tadakhul (categorical/hamli), qiyas talazum (conditional conjunctive), qiyas ta'anud (conditional disjunctive). Note the Arabic names and their meanings.
2
Understand the logic behind the names
Why 'overlap'? In categorical, subject and predicate overlap. 'Entailment' for conditional conjunctive because the consequent follows. 'Opposition' for disjunctive because the disjuncts are opposed.
3
Recognize the five matters
The passage refers to 'the five previously mentioned arts'. Recall from earlier lessons: burhani (demonstrative), jadali (dialectical), khatabi (rhetorical), shi'ri (poetical), safsati (sophistical).
These five matters correspond to the five arts of logic.
4
Apply the classification
Given a syllogism, determine its form (categorical or conditional) and its matter (based on the epistemic status of its premises). For example, an argument using certain premises is demonstrative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صور الأقيسة القياس - باعتبار صورته - إلى «قياس تداخل» وهو الحَمْلى، و«قياسُ تلازم» وهو الشرطي المتصل، و«قياس تعاند»، وهو التقسيم والترديد، وهو الشرطي المنفصل. هذا باعتبار صورته. وباعتبار مادته إلى الأصناف الخمسة المتقدمة. أقسام القياس فلا بد من الكلام في مواد «القياس»؛ وهي القضايا التي يستدل بها على غيرها. باعتبار الطرق هذا كله في قياس الشُّمُول. وأما «قياس التمثيل» و «الاستقراء» فله حكم آخر. فإنهم قالوا : الاستدلال بـ «الكلّي» على «الجُزْئي» هو قياس الشمول؛ وبـ «الجزئي» على «الكلّي» هو «الاستقراء»، إما «التام» إن عُلم شموله للأفراد، وإلا فـ «الناقص»؛ والاستدلال بأحد الجُزئيين على الآخر هو «قياس التمثيل»
The forms of syllogisms: the syllogism – in terms of its form – into 'overlap syllogism' which is the categorical, 'entailment syllogism' which is the conditional conjunctive, and 'opposition syllogism' which is the disjunctive conditional. This is in terms of its form. And in terms of its matter, into the five previously mentioned arts. The divisions of the syllogism: it is necessary to discuss the materials of the syllogism; they are the propositions by which one infers to other things. In terms of methods, all of this falls under the 'comprehensive syllogism'. As for the 'analogical syllogism' and 'induction', they have a different ruling. For they said: inference from the universal to the particular is the comprehensive syllogism; inference from the particular to the universal is induction – either 'complete' if its coverage of all individuals is known, otherwise 'incomplete'; and inference from one particular to another is 'analogical syllogism'.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage defines the basic classification of syllogisms (qiyas) in logic. The author states that in terms of form, qiyas is divided into three: the categorical (hamli), also called 'overlap syllogism' because the subject and predicate overlap; the conditional conjunctive (sharti muttasil), called 'entailment syllogism' because the consequent follows from the antecedent; and the conditional disjunctive (sharti munfasil), also called 'division syllogism'. In terms of matter, qiyas is divided into the five arts (demonstrative, dialectical, rhetorical, poetical, sophistical). Then the author explains that there are three types of inference derived from the direction of reasoning: 1) from universal to particular is qiyas shumul (comprehensive syllogism), 2) from particular to universal is istiqra' (induction), which is either complete (tamm) if all individuals are examined, or incomplete (naqis), and 3) from one particular to another is qiyas tamthil (analogical syllogism). This classification is foundational for understanding the logicians' framework. The student must memorize these categories and their definitions.

Key Terms
القياس
قياس التداخل
قياس التلازم
قياس التعاند
الحملى
الشرطي المتصل
الشرطي المنفصل

A) Two
B) Three
C) Five
D) Ten

Key Takeaway
Qiyas is divided by form into three (categorical, conditional conjunctive, conditional disjunctive) and by matter into five arts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the following qiyas by form: 'If the world is temporally originated, it has a cause; the world is temporally originated; therefore it has a cause.' (Answer: conditional conjunctive/sharti muttasil)
?
Classify the following qiyas by matter: 'Every khamr is intoxicating; every intoxicant is forbidden; so every khamr is forbidden.' Assume the premises are from the Qur'an and Sunnah (certain). (Answer: demonstrative/burhani)

Three Directions of Inference: Qiyas al-Shumul, Istiqra', Qiyas al-Tamthil
Author defines three types of inference based on the direction of reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between qiyas al-shumul, istiqra', and qiyas al-tamthil based on the direction of reasoning (from universal to particular, particular to universal, particular to particular).

Core Idea
All inference moves from known to unknown; the direction determines whether it is deduction, induction, or analogy.

This passage introduces three fundamental types of inference recognized by the logicians. The direction of reasoning – whether from universal to particular, particular to universal, or particular to particular – defines each type. This distinction is essential for understanding how arguments work in Islamic sciences, from fiqh rulings to theological proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three inference types
Find in the Arabic: qiyas al-shumul (inference from universal to particular), al-istiqra' (particular to universal), qiyas al-tamthil (particular to particular). Mark the Arabic phrases.
2
Analyze the direction of reasoning
For each type, note which term is the starting point (known) and which is the target (unknown). In qiyas al-shumul, we start with a universal rule and apply it to a particular instance. In istiqra', we go from many particulars to a universal. In tamthil, we go from one particular to another.
3
Understand subcategories of istiqra'
The passage mentions 'tamm' (complete) and 'naqis' (incomplete) induction. Complete induction covers all individuals; incomplete induction covers only some and yields probability.
4
Differentiate from form classification
Note that these three types are not the same as the three forms. Qiyas al-shumul can be categorical or conditional; it's the direction that defines it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

This passage defines the basic classification of syllogisms (qiyas) in logic. The author states that in terms of form, qiyas is divided into three: the categorical (hamli), also called 'overlap syllogism' because the subject and predicate overlap; the conditional conjunctive (sharti muttasil), called 'entailment syllogism' because the consequent follows from the antecedent; and the conditional disjunctive (sharti munfasil), also called 'division syllogism'. Ta'thir vs. In'ikas: Invalidating a Cause
Author refines the understanding of causation (illa), showing that lack of convertibility does not invalidate a cause, only lack of effect does.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between 'adam al-ta'thir (lack of effect) and 'adam al-in'ikas (lack of convertibility) and determine which invalidates a claimed cause according to Ibn Taymiyya.

Core Idea
Only lack of effect (ta'thir) invalidates a cause; lack of convertibility (in'ikas) does not.

In this hasheya, Ibn Taymiyya sharpens the criteria for what makes a valid cause in Islamic legal reasoning. He confronts a common error: mistaking a cause's failure to produce a ruling in every instance (lack of convertibility) for its ineffectiveness. Using legal examples from criminal law and inheritance, he shows that multiple causes can yield the same generic ruling, and only the absence of effect—not convertibility—truly undermines a cause.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's confusion
The objector claims that attributes like being married have no effect because the same ruling (licit blood) occurs without them (e.g., through apostasy). This confuses lack of effect with multiple causation.
The objector assumes that if a ruling exists without a specific attribute, that attribute cannot be a cause.
2
Analyze Ibn Taymiyya's response
He distinguishes between the generic ruling and its specific types. Blood permissible through apostasy, adultery, and murder are different types with different consequences. Similarly, inheritance via lineage, marriage, and clientage are distinct causes for the same generic ruling.
He provides clear analogies from Islamic law to illustrate that the same generic effect can have multiple causes.
3
Understand the principle: ta'thir vs. in'ikas
Lack of effect (adam al-ta'thir) occurs when the ruling exists without the cause and no other cause explains it. Lack of convertibility (adam al-in'ikas) occurs when the ruling is absent but the cause is present—this does not invalidate the cause.
Ibn Taymiyya explicitly states: 'the proof (dalil) does not require convertibility'—only definitions require convertibility.
4
Apply the distinction to legal reasoning
In qiyas (analogical deduction), a cause need not be convertible; it only needs to be effective in producing its ruling in the cases where it is present. This is a departure from logicians who demand necessary and sufficient conditions.
This refinement protects many valid analogies from being rejected due to incomplete convertibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۱) . . . فإنها إذا انتفت انتفى الحكم المعلق بها، وإذا وجد الحكم بعلة أخرى، فإن كانت العلة مساوية لها من كل وجه فهى هي، وإن كانت مجانبة لها في بعض الـ . فلا بد أن يختلف [الحكم]، كما أن حلّ الدم كان اسم جنس، فالحل الحاصل بالرِدَّة نوع غير النوع الحاصل بالزّنا والحل الحاصل بالقتل. فإن حِلَّ الدم الحاصل [بالقتل] يجوز فيه الفداء والمعافاة، والحلَّ الحاصل بالرّدة [ يجوز] فيه الغُفْران بالتوبة، والحلَّ الحاصل بالزنا فيه حد الرجم بالحجارة وبحضور شهود وكثير نظائر هذا. فإلى ... في أحد الحِلَّين إن كان مماثلاً للآخر فالعلتان [واحدة]، وإن اختلف الحكم اختلفت [العلة]. وانتقاض الوضوء بالبول والغائط نوع واحد ليس هو من باب تغاير الحكم بعلتين . . . كالقتل بالحدود. قال المعترض : هذه الأوصاف لا تأثير لها في حِلّ الدّم، فإن الردّة و زنا المحصن ... وحراب الكافر الأصلي يبيح الدّم مع انتفاء هذه الأوصاف . فيقال له : فإن الحلّ وُجد لسبب آخر لا لعدم تأثير هذه الأوصاف، كما يستحق الابن الإرث بالنسب والنكاح والولاء ويثبتُ المِلْك بالمعاوضة والإرث والإيهاب والاغتنام. وتلك المباحات لو كان للمستدلّ ... فيجب القود قياساً على القتل لم يرد عليه هذ السؤال، لأن الواجب بالردّة والزنا ليس قودا . وأما عدم التأثير مبطل لما هو العلة، لأن تأثير الدّلالة الذي يجمع فيه لا يدلّ على العلة، فإن الدليل لا يجب انعكاسه، كما لو قال من يركب القياس في مسألة زكاة الصبي :
Then, if it (the cause) is absent, the ruling attached to it is absent; and if the ruling exists due to another cause, then if the cause is equivalent in every respect, it is the same, but if it differs in some ... then the [ruling] must differ, just as the permissibility of blood was a generic term, so the permissibility obtained through apostasy is a type different from that obtained through adultery and from the permissibility obtained through murder. For the permissibility of blood [obtained through murder] may allow ransom and pardon, and the permissibility through apostasy may allow forgiveness by repentance, and the permissibility through adultery involves stoning with stones in the presence of witnesses, and many [are] similar to this. So until ... if one of the two permissibilities is similar to the other, then the causes are [one], and if the ruling differs, the [cause] differs. The nullification of ablution through urine and feces is one type and is not of the category of difference of ruling by two causes ... like killing by fixed punishments. The objector said: These attributes have no effect on the permissibility of blood, for apostasy and adultery of a married person ... and highway robbery of the original unbeliever permit blood despite the absence of these attributes. So it is said to him: Then the permissibility exists due to another reason, not due to the lack of effect of these attributes, just as the son deserves inheritance through lineage, marriage, and clientage, and ownership is established through exchange, inheritance, gift, and spoils. And those permissible acts, if the objector... then retaliation must be made by analogy with murder, but this question does not apply, because what is obligatory through apostasy and adultery is not retaliation. And as for lack of effect, it invalidates what is the cause, because the effect of the indication which encompasses it does not indicate the cause, for the proof does not require convertibility, as when one who uses analogy in the issue of the zakat of a minor...
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This hasheya (margin note) from Ibn Taymiyya refines the understanding of causality ('illa). He begins by stating a principle: when the cause is absent, the ruling (hukm) attached to it is absent; but if the ruling exists due to another cause, then the causes must be distinguished. He gives examples from Islamic law: the permissibility of shedding blood (halal al-dam) can arise from apostasy, adultery, or murder, but each is a distinct type with different consequences (ransom, forgiveness, stoning). This shows that the same generic ruling can have different causes. Then he presents an objection: an objector says that certain attributes (like being a married person) have no effect because blood becomes licit even without them (e.g., apostasy). The response is that the permissibility arises from a different cause, not because the attributes are ineffective. He uses analogies from inheritance law and modes of ownership to illustrate that multiple causes can produce the same effect. Crucially, he distinguishes between lack of effect (adam al-ta'thir), which truly invalidates a cause, and lack of convertibility (adam al-in'ikas), which does not. He concludes that a proof (dalil) does not require convertibility, unlike a definition. This is a sophisticated critique of the logicians' demand for necessary and sufficient conditions in causal reasoning.

Key Terms
علة ('illa): A cause that brings about a ruling (hukm). Its validity is determined by its effect (ta'thir), not by convertibility. [Note: In Usul al-Fiqh, 'illa is typically defined as a descriptive attribute that indicates a ruling. Here Ibn Taymiyya emphasizes that causes can be multiple for the same generic ruling.]
عدم التأثير ('adam al-ta'thir): Lack of effect: when an attribute is claimed to be the cause but the ruling exists without it, proving it is not the actual cause.
عدم الانعكاس ('adam al-in'ikas): Lack of convertibility: the ruling does not always disappear when the cause is absent. This does not invalidate the cause, unlike ta'thir. [Note: In logic, in'ikas is required for definitions; here Ibn Taymiyya argues it is not required for causes.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya in the hasheya, what truly invalidates a claimed cause ('illa)?
A) Absence of the ruling when the cause is absent (adam al-in'ikas)
B) Existence of the ruling without the cause while no other cause exists (adam al-ta'thir)
C) The ruling being more general than the cause
D) The cause being too specific

Key Takeaway
Only lack of effect (adam al-ta'thir), not lack of convertibility (adam al-in'ikas), invalidates a cause in Islamic legal reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In a debate on riba, a jurist claims that the 'illa of exchange is hand-to-hand delivery. An objector says the ruling of riba does not apply when goods are of the same type but exchanged on credit, because the cause (hand-to-hand) is absent. Does this objection target ta'thir or in'ikas? Justify using Ibn Taymiyya's framework.
?
Consider the hadd punishment for theft: the cause is unlawful taking of property (qat' al-yad). If a person steals but the hand is not cut due to repentance, does this invalidate the cause? Explain why or why not based on the lesson.

Introduction to the Negative Stance on Syllogism
Introducing the refutation of the claim that all tasdiqat require syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two-station structure Ibn Taymiyya uses to refute the claim that all knowledge of assents requires syllogism.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya's critique of syllogism begins by separating negative refutation from positive alternative.

In this brief passage, Ibn Taymiyya announces a new section in his refutation of the logicians' central claim. He outlines two stations: a negative station (which denies the necessity of syllogism for all assents) and a positive station (which will present an alternative). This structural division is key to understanding his method.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being refuted
Find the statement that Ibn Taymiyya attributes to the logicians: 'Nothing of assents is known except through syllogism.' This is the target of his negative stance.
This claim is the central dogma about syllogism that he will challenge.
2
Locate the two stations
The passage mentions 'al-maqām al-salbī' (the negative station) and hints at a second station. Notice that he says 'the discussion is in two stations as well' — he will address both negative and positive aspects.
The negative station comes first; the positive station is not developed here.
3
Recognize the purpose of the negative station
Ibn Taymiyya begins his refutation by stating 'so we say' (fa naqūl), indicating that the negative argument will follow. This station aims to show that the logicians' claim is false.
The passage cuts off, so the actual negative argument appears in subsequent sections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثالث
المقام السلبي في الأقيسة والتصديقات
في قولهم : إنه لا يُعلم شيء من التصديقات إلا بالقياس
فصل
وأما «القياس»، فالكلام في المقامين أيضاً. المقام الأول : السَّلْبي . فنقول :
The third station: The negative station concerning syllogisms and assents. Regarding their statement: 'Nothing of assents is known except through syllogism.' Section: As for syllogism, the discussion is in two stations as well. The first station: the negative. So we say:
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage is a brief introduction to a new section where Ibn Taymiyya begins his refutation of the logicians' claim that all certain knowledge (taṣdīqāt) is obtained only through syllogism (qiyās). He announces that the discussion will have two stations (maqām): a negative station (which refutes the claim) and a positive station (which likely establishes an alternative). Here he only begins the negative station with 'fa naqūl' (so we say), but the text cuts off. This sets up the structure for the following arguments.

Key Terms
القياس
التصديقات
المقام السلبي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He accepts that syllogism is necessary for all assents.
B) He will present a negative argument against the claim that all assents depend on syllogism.
C) He will discuss the negative mood of the syllogism.
D) He will define what syllogism is not.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya begins his critique of syllogism by distinguishing a negative refutation from a positive alternative, a method common in Islamic critical works.

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
Ibn Taymiyya's Refutation of the Unproven Negative Claim
Ibn Taymiyya attacks the claim that all tasdiqat require syllogism as unsubstantiated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a universal negative claim that lacks proof and explain why it fails logically, using Ibn Taymiyya's critique as a model.

Core Idea
A universal negative claim without proof is no better than ignorance.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya targets the logicians' blanket assertion that no affirmation is known except through their specific syllogism. He shows that this universal negative statement is neither self-evident nor supported by any evidence, making it an unjustified claim. This is a key moment in his larger project to dismantle the privileged status of Greek logical formalism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
Locate the logicians' statement: 'No affirmation is known except by syllogism.' Recognize it as a universal negative proposition (qadiyya salbiyya nāfiya) that denies something about all affirmations.
This claim is not about particular cases but a sweeping universal.
2
Assess its epistemic status
Ibn Taymiyya points out that it is not known by intuition (badīha) and the logicians provided no proof (dalīl). Thus it is unsubstantiated.
3
Recognize the burden of proof
A universal negative claim requires evidence. The logicians have failed to provide any, so they are 'speakers without knowledge'.
This follows the principle that a claim without evidence is not to be accepted.
4
Apply the critique
Consider the self-referential problem: if all knowledge requires syllogism, how could they know this universal negative themselves? It would require a prior syllogism, leading to circularity or infinite regress.
Ibn Taymiyya implies this by saying 'knowledge of this negation is impossible according to their own principle'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثالث: المقام السلبي في الأقيسة والتصديقات: في قولهم : إنه لا يُعلم شيء من التصديقات إلا بالقياس. فصل وأما «القياس»، فالكلام في المقامين أيضاً. المقام الأول : السَّلْبي . فنقول : قولهم : إنه لا يُعلم شيء من التصديقات إلا بالقياس - الذي ذكروا صورته و مادته - قضيةٌ سَلْبية نافية ليست معلومة بالبديهة. ولم يذكروا على هذا السلب دليلاً أصلاً، فصاروا مُدَّعِين ما لم يبينوه، بل قائلين بغير علم؛ إذ العلم بهذا السلب متعذر على أصلهم. فمن أين لهم أنه لا يمكن أحداً من بني آدم أن يعلم شيئاً من التصديقات - التي ليست بديهية عندهم - إلا بواسطة القياس المنطقي الشمولي» - الذي وصفوا مادته وصورته؟
The third station: The negative station concerning syllogisms and affirmations: regarding their statement: 'No affirmation is known except by syllogism.' Section: As for 'syllogism', the discussion is also in two stations. The first station: the negative. So we say: Their statement: 'No affirmation is known except by syllogism' – which mentioned its form and matter – is a negative, denying proposition not known by intuition. And they did not mention any proof for this negation at all, so they became claimants of what they did not demonstrate, nay, speakers without knowledge; for knowledge of this negation is impossible according to their own principle. So from where do they have that no one among the children of Adam can know any of the affirmations – which are not intuitive according to them – except through the universal logical syllogism – whose matter and form they described?
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the logicians' claim that all knowledge of affirmations (taṣdīqāt) comes only through syllogism (qiyās). Ibn Taymiyya points out that this is a universal negative proposition (qadiyya salbiyya nāfiya). Such a claim is not self-evident (badīha), and the logicians have provided no proof for it. Therefore, they are making an unsubstantiated assertion—speaking without knowledge. Moreover, according to their own epistemological principles, knowing this negative universal would itself be impossible unless it is known through a syllogism, which would lead to circularity or infinite regress. He ends with a rhetorical question challenging the origin of their certainty that no human can know any non-intuitive affirmation without the specific logical syllogism they prescribe.

Key Terms
قياس منطقي شمولي
تصديقات
قضية سلبية نافية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does Ibn Taymiyya consider the logicians' claim 'speaking without knowledge'?
A) Because they contradicted themselves.
B) Because they made a negative claim without proof.
C) Because they used complex terminology.
D) Because they rejected revelation.

Key Takeaway
A universal negative claim without evidence is epistemically void; the burden of proof lies with the claimant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find a universal negative claim in a contemporary argument about Islamic law (e.g., 'No authentic hadith supports this view'). Test whether it meets Ibn Taymiyya's criteria: is it self-evident or proven? If not, how would you critique it?
?
Using the same structure, write a short critique of the claim 'All non-Arabic speakers cannot understand the Quran's miraculous nature'.

Necessary Properties and the Fallacy of the Dhati/Lazim Distinction
Ibn Taymiyya on necessary properties known without a middle term

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that the logicians' distinction between essential and necessary properties is invalid because both can be known without a middle term given complete conceptualization.

Core Idea
Complete conceptualization of subject and predicate renders the dhati/lazim distinction irrelevant.

Ibn Taymiyya turns to the logicians' claim that some necessary properties (lawaazim) are known without a middle term, while others require one. He observes that people differ profoundly in conceptual power. Those who achieve complete conceptualization of the terms involved can see the necessary entailments directly, without needing a separate proof. This undermines the logicians' neat separation of essential (dhati) and necessary (lazim) properties, since both become equally knowable through complete tasawwur.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
Ibn Taymiyya states that some necessary properties are established without a middle term (i.e., without a syllogistic proof). Look for the phrase 'ما ثبت بغير وسط'.
This is the starting point of his argument.
2
Notice the role of conceptualization
He links the direct knowledge of necessary properties to the quality of tasawwur (conceptualization) of the two terms (subject and predicate). People vary in their power of conceptualization.
The variation in conceptual ability is key to his critique.
3
Understand the distinction
The logicians distinguish between essential (dhati) and necessary (lazim) properties. Ibn Taymiyya argues that this distinction collapses because both can be known directly if conceptualization is complete.
He does not accept the ontological basis of the distinction.
4
Follow the reasoning
If one conceptualizes the subject and predicate by their common attributes (not just distinctive ones), one can know many necessary properties without a middle term.
This is the core logical move.
5
Draw the conclusion
Therefore, the logicians' distinction between dhati and lazim is invalid; the need for a middle term depends on conceptual ability, not the nature of the property.
This conclusion has epistemological implications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثالث - من «اللوازم» ما ثبت بغير «وسط»
وهو الدليل الذي هو «الحدّ الأوسط» - سواء كان تصور الطرفين «بديهياً» أو لم يكن. ومعلوم أن الناس يتفاوتون في قوى الأذهان أعظم من تفاوتهم في قوى الأبدان، ويتفاوتون في حسن التصور وتمامه وفي سوء التصور ونقصه. فمن الناس من يكون في سرعة التصور وجودته في غاية يباين بها غيره مباينة كبيرة. وحينئذ فيتصور الطرفين تصوراً تاماً بحيث تتبين بذلك التصور التامّ «اللوازم» التي لا تتبين لغيره الذي لم يتصور الطرفين التصور التام.
وذلك أن من الناس من يكون لم يتصور الطرفين إلا ببعض صفاتهما «المميّزة»، فيكون من تصورها ببعض صفاتهما المشتركة مع ذلك - سواء سميت «ذاتية» أو لم تسم - عالماً بثبوت تلك الصفات لهما وثبوتِ كثير مما يكون «لازماً» لهما، بخلاف من لم يتصور إلا الصفات المميّزة.
بطلان التفريق بين «الذاتي» و «اللازم» لثبوت كل منهما بغير «وسط»
وذلك أنهم قرروا في المنطق أن من اللوازم ما يكون «لازماً» بغير «وسط»، فهذا يعلم بنفس تصور «اللزوم».
المراد بالحد الأوسط
و «الوسط» المذكور في هذه المواضع هو عند ابن سينا ومحققيهم هو «الدليل»، وهو «الحد الأوسط». وهذا يختلف باختلاف الناس؛ فقد يحتاج هذا في العلم بـ «اللزوم» إلى فهم المراد بخلاف الآخر.
ومن لم يفهم مرادهم في هذا الموضع يظن أنهم أرادوا بـ «الوسط» ما هو وسط في نفس الموصوف بحيث يكون ثبوت الوصف اللازم للملزوم بواسطته لا يثبت بنفسه، كما قد فهم ذلك عنهم طائفة أنه غلط منهم الرازي وغيره. وهذا، مع أنه غلط منهم كما بينا ألفاظهم في غير هذا الموضع، فهو أيضاً باطل في نفسه.
ولا ريب أن من «اللوازم» ما يفتقر إلى «وسط»، ومنها ما لا يفتقر إلى «وسط»
The third station: Among necessary properties, what is established without a 'middle term'. And the 'middle term' is the proof, which is the 'middle term' – whether the conceptualization of the two ends is intuitive or not. It is known that people differ in the powers of their minds more than they differ in the powers of their bodies, and they differ in the excellence and completeness of conceptualization and in its deficiency and fault. So among people there are those whose quickness and quality of conceptualization is such that they vastly differ from others. Then they conceptualize the two ends completely such that the necessary properties become apparent through that complete conceptualization, which are not apparent to someone else who did not conceptualize the two ends completely.
This is because some people may conceptualize the two ends only by some of their distinctive attributes, while one who conceptualizes them by some of their common attributes – whether called essential or not – knows the affirmation of those attributes and the affirmation of much that is necessary for them, unlike one who conceptualizes only the distinctive attributes.
The invalidity of the distinction between the essential and the necessary, since each of them is established without a middle term.
For they established in logic that some necessary properties are necessary without a middle term, so this is known by the very conceptualization of the necessity.
The meaning of the middle term
And the 'middle term' mentioned in these contexts is, according to Ibn Sina and their verifiers, the 'proof', which is the 'middle term'. And this differs according to people; for this one may need, in knowing the necessary property, to understand the intended meaning, unlike the other.
And whoever does not understand their intention in this place thinks that they meant by 'middle term' something that is a middle in the very described thing, such that the affirmation of the necessary attribute to the subject is by means of it and is not established by itself, as a group understood from them that it is an error from al-Razi and others. And this, besides being an error from them as we have clarified their words elsewhere, is also false in itself.
And there is no doubt that among necessary properties there are those that depend on a middle term and those that do not depend on a middle term.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the logicians' treatment of necessary properties (lawaazim). Ibn Taymiyya argues that the distinction between essential (dhati) and necessary (lazim) properties is invalid because both can be known without a middle term if the conceptualization is complete. He emphasizes that the middle term (al-wasat) is actually a proof (dalil), and its need varies among people. He then points out a misunderstanding by al-Razi and others, who mistakenly thought that the middle term is something inherent in the described thing itself, whereas it is actually a proof that may be needed by some but not others. The passage illustrates the relativity of logical tools and the importance of complete conceptualization.

Key Terms
اللوازم
الذاتي
الوسط
التصور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, what determines whether a necessary property requires a middle term to be known?
A) Whether it is essential (dhati) or necessary (lazim)
B) The completeness of conceptualization of the subject and predicate
C) The type of syllogism used
D) The number of premises

Key Takeaway
The need for a middle term is relative to the knower's conceptual ability, not an intrinsic feature of the property.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the attribute of God's power (qudra). According to the logicians, is it dhati or lazim? Using Ibn Taymiyya's argument, explain how complete conceptualization of God (as the Necessary Being) directly yields the knowledge that He is powerful.
?

8 min
The Middle Term as Proof: Critique of al-Razi's Misunderstanding
Ibn Taymiyya's critique of al-Razi on the middle term

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Taymiyya's critique of al-Razi's misunderstanding of the middle term and his own definition of the middle term as a proof that varies among people.

Core Idea
The middle term is a proof, not an inherent intermediary; its necessity is subjective.

Ibn Taymiyya clarifies what the logicians – especially Ibn Sina – mean by the 'middle term' (al-wasat): it is the proof (al-dalil), not some inherent part of the thing being described. He then points out that al-Razi and others mistakenly thought that the middle term is a middle in the thing itself, such that the necessary attribute is established through it. Ibn Taymiyya rejects this as both a misinterpretation of the logicians' own words and false in itself. In reality, the need for a middle term depends on the individual knower; some may see the necessity directly, others require the proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify Ibn Taymiyya's definition
He quotes Ibn Sina and the verifiers: the 'middle term' is the proof (al-dalil). Look for the phrase 'هو الدليل'.
This is a direct correction of common misunderstanding.
2
Recognize the variability
The proof differs among people; one person may need it to see the necessity, another may not. This is linked to conceptual ability.
Ibn Taymiyya emphasizes the relativity of logical tools.
3
Understand the misunderstanding
al-Razi and some others thought the middle term is a middle in the described thing itself (i.e., an intrinsic intermediary that carries the attribute).
This is a reification of the term.
4
Analyze the critique
Ibn Taymiyya says this misunderstanding is both an error in reading the logicians and false in itself. The falsity stems from the fact that necessary properties can be known directly without an intermediary when conceptualization is complete.
He does not elaborate extensively but asserts the falsehood.
5
Conclude
Therefore, the middle term is a proof, and its necessity is not a fixed ontological feature but depends on the knower.
This aligns with his broader epistemological project.

Source Text

The passage illustrates the relativity of logical tools and the importance of complete conceptualization.

The Syllogism
advanced
10 min
Refutation of the Necessity of a Universal Premise in Demonstration
Ibn Taymiyya argues that requiring a universal premise in demonstration leads to infinite regress or circularity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument against the necessity of a universal premise in demonstration and evaluate its logical force.

Core Idea
If every demonstration requires a universal premise, the premise itself must be known, leading to circularity or infinite regress.

Logicians claim that demonstration (burhan) must have a universal affirmative premise. Ibn Taymiyya challenges this, arguing that such a requirement creates an epistemic regress. In this lesson, we walk through his critique and see why it threatens the foundational role of universal premises in certainty.

Guided Path
1
State the logicians' claim
Identify the claim that demonstration requires a universal affirmative premise. Logicians assert that certain knowledge comes only through such premises.
This is the target of Ibn Taymiyya's refutation.
2
Identify the two possibilities for knowing that premise
If a universal premise is required, its universality must be known. This knowledge is either self-evident (badihi) or theoretical (nadari).
These are the only two epistemic categories.
3
Analyze the self-evident option
If the premise is self-evident, then each of its individual instances would also be self-evident. But this is false because we do not have self-evident knowledge of every instance.
For example, the universal 'every intoxicant is forbidden' would require self-evident knowledge of every intoxicant.
4
Analyze the theoretical option
If the premise is theoretical, it requires another premise to prove it, leading to either circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul). Both are impossible for created human knowledge, which has a beginning.
This regress is not like past events; it is a regress in justifications.
5
Conclude the refutation
Since both possibilities lead to impossibility, the original requirement that demonstration must have a universal premise is false.
Ibn Taymiyya thus undermines a core tenet of Aristotelian logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بطلان دعواهم : لا بد في «البرهان» من «قضية كلية» .وأيضاً، فإذا قالوا : العلوم اليقينية النظرية لا تحصل إلا بـ «البرهان»، الذي عندهم قياس شمولي». وعندهم لا بد فيه من «قضية كلية موجبة» . ولهذا قالوا إنه لا نتاج عن قضيتين سالبتين) ولا جزئيتين» في شيء من أنواع القياس، لا بحسب صورته ، كـ «الحَمْلي»، و «الشرطي المتصل»، و «المنفصل»؛ ولا مادته، لا «البرهاني»، ولا «الخَطَابي»، ولا «الجدلي»، بل ولا بحسب «الشعري». فيقال : إذا كان لا بد في كل ما يسمونه «برهاناً» من «قضية كلية فلا بد من العلم يكون بتلك القضية الكلية، أي من العلم بكونها «كلية» . وإلا ، فمتى جُوّز عليها أن لا تكون كلية) بل جزئية لم يحصل العلم بموجبها . و «المهملة» ـ وهي المطلقة التي يحتمل لفظها أن تكون كلية ) و (جزئية» - في قوة «الجزئية» .وإذا كان لا بد في العلم الحاصل بالقياس - الذي يخصونه باسم «البرهان» - من العلم بـ «قضية كلية موجبة»، فيقال: العلم بتلك القضية إن كان بديهياً أمكن أن يكون كلُّ واحد من أفرادها «بديهيا» بطريق الأولى. وإن كان نظرياً» احتاج إلى علم بديهي فيفضي إلى الدور المَعِي» أو التسلسل في أمور لها مبدأ محدود». فإن علم ابن آدم إذا توقف على علم منه، وعلمه على علم منه ، فعلمه له مبدأ، لأنه نفسه له مبدأ ; ليس هذا كـَ تسلسل الحوادث الماضية ; وأيضاً فإنه تسلسل في «المؤثرات». وكلاهما (۱) باطل .
Refutation of their claim: The 'demonstration' necessarily requires a 'universal proposition'. Also, if they say: The certain theoretical sciences are not obtained except through 'demonstration', which according to them is a 'comprehensive syllogism'. And according to them it necessarily requires a 'universal affirmative proposition'. And for this reason they said that nothing results from two negative premises nor from two particular premises in any type of syllogism, neither in terms of its form, like the 'categorical', the 'conditional conjunctive', and the 'disjunctive'; nor in terms of its matter, neither the 'demonstrative', nor the 'rhetorical', nor the 'dialectical', nor even the 'poetic'. So it is said: If every what they call 'demonstration' necessarily requires a 'universal proposition', then knowledge of that universal proposition must be obtained, i.e., knowledge that it is 'universal'. Otherwise, if it is possible that it is not universal but particular, then knowledge of its content is not obtained. And the 'indefinite'—which is the absolute whose expression could be universal or particular—has the force of the 'particular'. And if the knowledge obtained by the syllogism—which they specifically call 'demonstration'—necessarily requires knowledge of a 'universal affirmative proposition', it is said: The knowledge of that proposition: if it is self-evident, then it is possible that each one of its individuals be 'self-evident' a fortiori. And if it is theoretical, it needs a self-evident knowledge, leading to 'concurrent circularity' or 'regress' in things that have a limited beginning. For the knowledge of the son of Adam, if it depends on some knowledge, and that knowledge depends on another knowledge, then his knowledge has a beginning, because he himself has a beginning; this is not like the regress of past events; and also it is a regress in 'efficient causes'. And both (circularity and regress) are false.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the logicians' claim that demonstration (burhan) necessarily requires a universal affirmative premise. He points out that if such a premise is required, then one must know that it is universal. If it is self-evident, then each of its individuals would be self-evident (which is absurd). If it is theoretical, it would require another premise, leading to circularity or infinite regress. Since human knowledge has a beginning (as humans are created), such a regress is impossible. Therefore, the logicians' requirement of a universal premise in all demonstration is false.

قضية كلية
دور
تسلسل

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya demonstrates that the logicians' requirement of a universal premise in demonstration is epistemologically impossible due to circularity or infinite regress.

Critique of Attributing Syllogistic Form to Prophetic Speech
Ibn Taymiyya examines the logicians' example of the syllogism about intoxicants and critiques the idea that the Prophet spoke in logical form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's critique of attributing formal logical syllogisms to prophetic sayings and evaluate its implications for using logic in Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
The Prophet's speech is too elevated for artificial logical form.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya examines a specific fiqh example—the prohibition of nabidh—to expose the fallacy of thinking that the Prophet spoke in the form of a categorical syllogism. He shows that the logical arrangement (al-nazm al-mantiqi) is not only unnecessary but beneath the dignity of Prophetic knowledge. This lesson will guide you through Ibn Taymiyya's deconstruction of the logicians' claim and help you apply his critique to similar arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim being attacked
The logicians claim that the Prophet's hadith 'Every intoxicant is khamr, and every khamr is forbidden' is a syllogism in logical form. Recognize that the logicians see this as a structured argument with major and minor premises leading to a conclusion.
This is the target of Ibn Taymiyya's critique.
2
Separate the uncontested premises from the contested one
Ibn Taymiyya points out that the major premise 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' is agreed upon, but the minor premise 'Nabidh is an intoxicant' is disputed. This shows that the logical form does not resolve the actual point of disagreement.
The dispute is factual, not about logical validity.
3
Examine Ibn Taymiyya's objection to attributing logical form to the Prophet
He says it is 'great ignorance' to think the Prophet used such a method. Ibn Taymiyya argues that the Prophet is far too great to need artificial syllogistic reasoning; even the weakest scholars of his Ummah disdain it.
This is an epistemological and dignity-based objection.
4
Assess the broader critique of logicians
Ibn Taymiyya states that logicians are skilled only in crafts like arithmetic and medicine, not in universal demonstrative sciences or divine sciences. He is attacking the competence of logicians in the very fields they claim to master.
This is a strong polemical move, not a purely logical argument.
5
Apply the critique to a similar example
Think of another Islamic legal ruling that is argued using syllogistic form, and see if Ibn Taymiyya's critique would apply. This helps internalize the lesson.
For example, the analogy for qiyas in usul al-fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مثال تحریم النبيذ وأما إذا أخذته من المواد المعلومة بأقوال الأنبياء فإنه يظهر الاحتياج إلى القضية الكلية. كما إذا أردنا بيان تحريم النَّبِيذ المتنازع فيه، فقلنا : «النبيذ مسكر» و «كل مسكر حرام . أو قلنا: إنه خمر» و «کل خمر حرام» . فقولنا النبيذ المسكر خمر يُعلم بالنصّ، وهو قول النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم : كل مسکر خمر. وقولنا كل خمر حرام يعلم بالنص والإجماع. وليس في ذلك نزاع، وإنما النزاع في «المقدمة الصغرى». وقد ثبت في صحيح مسلم عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال : كل مسكر خمر، وكل مسكر حرام (۱) . وفي لفظ : كل مسكر خمر، وكل خمر حرام»(۲). وقد يظن بعض الناس أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم ذكر هذا على النظم المنطقي ليبين النتيجة» بـ «المقدمتين» كما يفعله المنطقيون. وهذا جهل عظيم ممن يظنه. فإن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أجلُّ قَدْراً من أن يستعمل مثل هذه الطريق في بيان العلم . بل من هو أضعفُ عقلاً وعلماً من آحاد علماء أمته لا يرضى لنفسه أن يسلك طريقة هؤلاء المنطقيين. بل يعدونهم من الجهال الذين لا يحسنون الاستدال. ويقولون : هؤلاء قوم كانوا يحسنون الصناعات كالحساب والطب ونحو ذلك . وأما العلوم البرهانية الكلية اليقينية والعلوم الإلهية فلم يكونوا من رجالها .
Example of the prohibition of nabidh (date wine). And if you take it from matters known by the statements of the prophets, then the need for the universal premise becomes apparent. As if we want to explain the prohibition of the contested nabidh, so we say: 'Nabidh is an intoxicant' and 'Every intoxicant is forbidden'. Or we say: 'It is khamr' and 'Every khamr is forbidden'. So our statement 'Nabidh is an intoxicant' is known by the text, which is the saying of the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'Every intoxicant is khamr'. And our statement 'Every khamr is forbidden' is known by the text and consensus. There is no dispute about that; rather, the dispute is about the minor premise. And it is established in Sahih Muslim from the Prophet (peace be upon him) that he said: 'Every intoxicant is khamr, and every intoxicant is forbidden.' In another wording: 'Every intoxicant is khamr, and every khamr is forbidden.' And some people think that the Prophet (peace be upon him) mentioned this in a logical arrangement to explain the conclusion by means of the two premises, as the logicians do. This is great ignorance from the one who thinks it. For the Prophet (peace be upon him) is far too great to use such a method in explaining knowledge. Rather, even the weakest in intellect and knowledge among the individual scholars of his Ummah would not be pleased for himself to follow the way of these logicians. Rather, they consider them among the ignorant who do not know how to argue. And they say: 'These are people who were good at crafts like arithmetic, medicine, and the like. As for demonstrative universal certain sciences and divine sciences, they were not among their people.'
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage continues the critique by applying it to a specific fiqh example: the prohibition of nabidh. Ibn Taymiyya argues that when dealing with revealed matters, the need for a universal premise becomes apparent. For instance, to prove that nabidh is forbidden, one can argue: 'Nabidh is an intoxicant' (minor premise) and 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' (major premise). The major premise is known from the hadith 'Every intoxicant is khamr' (according to one wording), and the conclusion that khamr is forbidden is known by text and consensus. The dispute is only about the minor premise (whether nabidh is an intoxicant). He then cites the hadith from Sahih Muslim and notes that some people mistakenly think the Prophet uttered these words in the logical form (with 'every' and the syllogistic structure). Ibn Taymiyya rebukes this as profound ignorance. The Prophet is above needing such artificial methodology. Even the weakest scholars of his community would not resort to the logicians' way; they even consider these logicians as ignorant in demonstrative and divine sciences, being only skilled in mundane crafts.

Key Terms
المقدمة الصغرى (al-muqaddima al-sughra): The minor premise in a syllogism; in this example, 'Nabidh is an intoxicant', which is the point of dispute among jurists. [Note: Standard logical term; Ibn Taymiyya uses it to show that only the minor premise is contested, not the major.]
النظم المنطقي (al-nazm al-mantiqi): The logical arrangement of a syllogism into major and minor premises; Ibn Taymiyya rejects attributing this to prophetic speech. [Note: Ibn Taymiyya uses this term pejoratively to contrast with natural argumentation.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the Prophet was illiterate.
B) Because the hadith is not in that form and such form is beneath his rank.
C) Because the logicians are always correct.
D) Because the minor premise is disputed.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya argues that prophetic speech is direct and authoritative, needing no artificial logical arrangement, and that attributing syllogistic form to it demeans its status.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify another hadith that might be misinterpreted as a syllogism (e.g., 'Actions are by intentions' is a universal statement, but not a syllogism). Explain why Ibn Taymiyya would reject its characterization as logical form.

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
The Prophet's Universal Speech vs. Syllogistic Form
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the claim that the Prophet used logical syllogism format.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the claim that the Prophet's hadith follow the logical syllogism format.

Core Idea
The Prophet's universal statements are direct, not deduced from premises.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya refutes the logicians' claim that the Prophet's hadith about intoxicants are examples of syllogistic reasoning. He argues that the Prophet's statements are universal propositions (qadiyya kulliyya) given directly, not conclusions drawn from two premises. Understanding this clarifies the difference between prophetic knowledge and human logical deduction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' example
Locate the syllogism format that the logicians claim the Prophet used: 'Every intoxicant is khamr, and every khamr is haram, so every intoxicant is haram.' Note that Ibn Taymiyya presents this as their claim.
This is a categorical syllogism in the first figure.
2
Find Ibn Taymiyya's refutation of the format
Ibn Taymiyya says the hadith wording is not 'on the logical system' (naẓm manṭiqī). He asserts that the Prophet is too great to use such a method. Highlight the phrase 'This is immense ignorance from those who think it.'
3
Recognize the actual prophetic method
Ibn Taymiyya explains that the Prophet gave universal propositions directly, like 'Every intoxicant is haram' and 'Every intoxicant is khamr.' These are self-sufficient, not conclusions from a syllogism. Note the two universal propositions from Sahih Muslim.
The Prophet's answer is a universal statement, not a syllogism.
4
Understand the role of the minor premise
The only dispute (niza') is about the minor premise: whether a particular drink (like nabidh) is intoxicating or khamr. The major premise (every khamr is haram) is already known by text and consensus. So the syllogism is unnecessary for those who accept the Prophet's authority.
The minor premise is a matter of empirical fact or definition, not logical deduction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مثال تحریم النبيذ. لا يكون لفظ الحديث على النظم المنطقي. فإنه يظهر بها فساد منطقهم. كتاب الرد على المنطقيين الكلام على تمثيلهم: كل مسكر خمر، وكل خمر حرام، فكل مسكر حرام. وأما إذا أخذته من المواد المعلومة بأقوال الأنبياء فإنه يظهر الاحتياج إلى القضية الكلية. كما إذا أردنا بيان تحريم النَّبِيذ المتنازع فيه، فقلنا: «النبيذ مسكر» و «كل مسكر حرام» . أو قلنا: إنه خمر» و «کل خمر حرام» . فقولنا النبيذ المسكر خمر يُعلم بالنصّ، وهو قول النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم: کل مسکر خمر. وقولنا كل خمر حرام يعلم بالنص والإجماع. وليس في ذلك نزاع، وإنما النزاع في «المقدمة الصغرى». وقد ثبت في صحيح مسلم عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال: كل مسكر خمر، وكل مسكر حرام. وفي لفظ: كل مسكر خمر، وكل خمر حرام. وقد يظن بعض الناس أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم ذكر هذا على النظم المنطقي ليبين النتيجة بـ «المقدمتين» كما يفعله المنطقيون. وهذا جهل عظيم ممن يظنه. فإن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أجلُّ قَدْراً من أن يستعمل مثل هذه الطريق في بيان العلم. بل من هو أضعفُ عقلاً وعلماً من آحاد علماء أمته لا يرضى لنفسه أن يسلك طريقة هؤلاء المنطقيين. بل يعدونهم من الجهال الذين لا يحسنون الاستدال. ويقولون: هؤلاء قوم كانوا يحسنون الصناعات كالحساب والطب ونحو ذلك. وأما العلوم البرهانية الكلية اليقينية والعلوم الإلهية فلم يكونوا من رجالها. وقد بين ذلك نُظّار المسلمين في كتبهم، وبسطوا الكلام عليهم. وذلك أن كون كل خمر حراماً هو مما علمه المسلمون، فلا يحتاجون إلى معرفة ذلك بـ «القياس». وإنما شكّ بعضهم في أنواع من الأشربة المسكرة كالنبيذ المصنوع من العسل والحبوب وغير ذلك، كما في الصحيحين عن أبي موسى الأشعري، أنه قيل: يا رسول الله! عندنا شراب يُصنع من العسل يقال له (البتع)، وشراب يصنع من الدُّرَة يقال له (المِزْر). قال: وكان قد أوتى جوامع الكلم، فقال: كل مسكر حرام. فأجابهم صلى الله عليه وسلم بقضية كلية بين بها أن كل ما يُسكر فهو محرم. وبين أيضاً أن كل ما يُسكر فهو خمر. وهاتان قضیتان کلیتان صادقتان متطابقتان العلم بأيهما كان يوجب العلم بتحريم كل مسكر إذ ليس العلم بتحريم كل مسكر متوقفاً على العلم بهما. فإن من علم أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال كل مسكر حرام، وهو من المؤمنين به علم أن النبيذ المسكر حرام ولكن قد يحصل له الشك: هل أراد القدح العاشر أو أراد جنس المسكر؟ وهذا شك في مدلول قوله، فإذا علم مراده صلى الله عليه وسلم علم المطلوب. وكذلك إذا علم أن النبيذ خمر - والعلم بهذا أوكد في التحريم - فإن من يحلل النبيذ المتنازع فيه لا يسمّيه خمراً. فإذا علم بالنص أن «كل مسكر خمر» كان هذا وحده دليلاً على تحريم كل مسكر عند أهل الإيمان الذين يعلمون أن «الخمر محرّم». وأما من لم يعلم تحريم الخمر لكونه لم يؤمن بالرسول فهذا لا يستدلّ بنصه
Example of prohibiting nabidh. The wording of the hadith is not in the logical system. For through it appears the corruption of their logic. The book of refutation of the logicians, discussion of their example: Every intoxicant is khamr, and every khamr is forbidden, so every intoxicant is forbidden. But if you take it from the matters known by the statements of the prophets, the need for the universal proposition appears. As when we want to explain the prohibition of the disputed nabidh, we say: 'Nabidh is an intoxicant' and 'Every intoxicant is forbidden.' Or we say: 'It is khamr' and 'Every khamr is forbidden.' So our saying 'the intoxicating nabidh is khamr' is known by the text, and that is the saying of the Prophet ﷺ: 'Every intoxicant is khamr.' And our saying 'every khamr is forbidden' is known by the text and consensus. There is no dispute in that; the dispute is only in the minor premise. And it has been established in Sahih Muslim from the Prophet ﷺ that he said: 'Every intoxicant is khamr, and every intoxicant is forbidden.' And in another wording: 'Every intoxicant is khamr, and every khamr is forbidden.' And some people think that the Prophet ﷺ mentioned this according to the logical system to show the conclusion by the two premises as the logicians do. This is immense ignorance from those who think it. For the Prophet ﷺ is too great in rank to use such a method in explaining knowledge. Rather, one who is weaker in intellect and knowledge than the common scholars of his nation would not be pleased with himself to follow the way of these logicians. Rather, they consider them among the ignorant who do not know how to reason. And they say: These are people who excelled in crafts like arithmetic and medicine and the like. But as for demonstrative universal certain sciences and divine sciences, they were not among their men. And the scholars of Islam have explained that in their books and elaborated the discussion against them. That is because the fact that every khamr is forbidden is something the Muslims know, so they do not need to know it by syllogism. But some of them doubted about types of intoxicating drinks like nabidh made from honey, grains, and others, as in the two Sahihs from Abu Musa al-Ash'ari that it was said: O Messenger of Allah! We have a drink made from honey called al-bit' and a drink made from corn called al-mizr. He said: And he had been given comprehensive speech, so he said: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden.' So he answered them with a universal proposition by which he clarified that everything that intoxicates is prohibited. And he also clarified that everything that intoxicates is khamr. These are two universal propositions, both true and corresponding; knowledge by either of them necessitates knowledge of the prohibition of every intoxicant, since knowledge of the prohibition of every intoxicant is not dependent on knowledge of both of them. For whoever knows that the Prophet ﷺ said 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' and he is a believer in him, knows that intoxicating nabidh is forbidden, but he may have doubt: did he mean the tenth cup or did he mean the genus of intoxicant? This is doubt in the meaning of his statement, so if he knows his intention ﷺ, he knows the desired. Likewise, if he knows that nabidh is khamr — and knowledge of this is more emphatic in prohibition — then whoever permits the disputed nabidh does not call it khamr. So if he knows by the text that 'Every intoxicant is khamr', this alone is proof for the prohibition of every intoxicant among the people of faith who know that 'khamr is forbidden.' And as for one who does not know the prohibition of khamr because he does not believe in the Messenger, he does not use his text as evidence.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the logicians' claim that the Prophet's hadith follow their syllogistic form. Ibn Taymiyya begins with the example they use: 'Every intoxicant is khamr, every khamr is haram, so every intoxicant is haram.' He notes that when taken from prophetic statements, the universal premise becomes apparent. He then explains that the disputed issue is the minor premise (whether nabidh is intoxicating or khamr), not the major premise which is known by text and consensus. He cites the hadith 'Every intoxicant is khamr, and every intoxicant is haram' from Sahih Muslim, and notes that some mistakenly think the Prophet arranged this in logical form. Ibn Taymiyya calls this immense ignorance, asserting that the Prophet is far above using such a method. He argues that even common scholars disdain the logicians' approach. He then elaborates that the knowledge that every khamr is haram is already known to Muslims; they only need to know if a particular drink falls under that category. He gives the example of the companions asking about al-bit' and al-mizr, and the Prophet answering with a universal statement: 'Every intoxicant is haram.' He explains that the Prophet gave two universal propositions, each sufficient on its own to prove the prohibition. He clarifies the role of doubt in the meaning of the Prophet's words, not in the logical deduction. Finally, he distinguishes between believers who accept the text and non-believers who do not.

Key Terms
خمر
مسكر
حرام
قضية كلية
المقدمة الصغرى
النظم المنطقي

C) Because it uses technical logical terms.
D) Because it is not found in Sahih Muslim.

Key Takeaway
Prophetic hadith are authoritative universal statements, not syllogisms; the logicians' attempt to fit them into their format is misguided.

The Syllogism

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of the Khamr-Muskir Example
Ibn Taymiyya critiques the logicians' use of the example of khamr and muskir

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's objection to the logicians' use of the khamr/muskir example and explain why syllogistic form alone is insufficient without material content.

Core Idea
The utility of a syllogism depends on the material truth of its premises, not just its form.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges a common example used by Muslim logicians to illustrate categorical syllogisms. He argues that the premise 'Every intoxicant is wine' does not yield the desired conclusion unless combined with the ruling of prohibition. This critique highlights the limitation of formal logic divorced from substantive knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim
Logicians often use the syllogism: 'Every intoxicant is wine; wine is forbidden; therefore every intoxicant is forbidden.' They claim this shows syllogistic form alone produces certain knowledge.
This is an example of Barbara mood in the first figure.
2
Deconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's objection
Ibn Taymiyya considers a scenario: someone knows Muhammad is a prophet but does not know that wine is forbidden. For such a person, the premise 'Every intoxicant is wine' is useless because it does not connect to the prohibition. They need the premise 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' to derive the prohibition of wine.
The example assumes knowledge of the prohibition, which is precisely what is to be proven.
3
Identify the useful syllogism
The beneficial syllogism is: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; wine is an intoxicant; therefore wine is forbidden.' Here, the major premise already contains the ruling, so the syllogism merely applies it.
The conclusion is already implicit in the major premise.
4
Evaluate the critique's validity
Ibn Taymiyya's critique is valid: the logicians' example only works if the major premise 'wine is forbidden' is already accepted. Thus, syllogistic form alone does not produce new knowledge about the ruling; it only transfers the ruling if the terms are coextensive.
The critique underscores the need for substantive premises rooted in revelation or empirical investigation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخمر والمسكر متلازمان وإن علم أن محمداً رسول الله ولكن لم يعلم أنه حرم الخمر فهذا لا ينفعه قوله كل مسكر خمر بل ينفعه قوله كل مسكر حرام وحينئذ يعلم بهذا تحريم الخمر لأن الخمر والمسكر اسمان لمسمى واحد عند الشارع وهما متلازمان عنده في العموم والخصوص عند جمهور العلماء الذين يحرمون كل مسكر وليس المقصود هنا الكلام في تقرير المسألة الشرعية بل التنبيه على التمثيل فإن هذا المثال كثيراً ما يمثل به من صنف في المنطق من علماء المسلمين
Wine and every intoxicant are coextensive. And if he knows that Muhammad is the Messenger of God, but he does not know that He prohibited wine, then his saying 'Every intoxicant is wine' does not benefit him. Rather, his saying 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' benefits him, and thereby he knows the prohibition of wine, because 'wine' and 'intoxicant' are two names for one referent in the Law, and they are coextensive in his (the Lawgiver's) view in generality and particularity according to the majority of scholars who prohibit every intoxicant. The intention here is not to discuss the legal issue, but to draw attention to the example, for this example is frequently used by those who authored works in logic among Muslim scholars.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage is a critique of a standard example used by Muslim logicians. Ibn Taymiyya points out that the universal premise 'Every intoxicant is wine' is not beneficial by itself to someone who knows that Muhammad is a prophet but does not know that wine is prohibited. The person needs the premise 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' to derive the prohibition. Thus, the utility of the syllogism depends on the content of the major premise, not merely its form. Ibn Taymiyya notes that in Islamic law, 'khamr' and 'muskir' are coextensive terms according to those who prohibit all intoxicants, but his aim is not to settle the legal ruling; rather, it is to show that the example is only effective if the relevant ruling is already known. This undermines the logicians' claim that abstract syllogistic form alone yields certain knowledge.

Key Terms
متلازمان
الشارع

B) Because the premise does not include the ruling of prohibition
C) Because the person does not know what an intoxicant is
D) Because the premise is particular not universal

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's critique shows that syllogistic form alone is insufficient for certain knowledge; the material truth of premises, especially legal rulings, must be established independently.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Syllogism
advanced

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Abstract Forms and Burhān
Ibn Taymiyya attacks abstract forms and denies burhān's necessity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's critique of abstract syllogistic forms and evaluate his argument that neither rational nor prophetic knowledge requires the logicians' burhān.

Core Idea
Abstract forms are empty shells; real knowledge comes from specific matter.

Ibn Taymiyya launches a two-pronged attack on the core methodology of the logicians. First, he shows that their cherished abstract forms (like 'Every A is B') are epistemically hollow—they teach nothing about real particulars. Second, he argues that both rational knowledge (like sensory evidence) and revealed knowledge (from prophets) are fully sufficient without the elaborate machinery of demonstrative syllogism. This lesson walks you through his critique and its implications for Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' practice
The logicians use abstract forms such as 'Every A is B, every B is C, so every A is C' stripped of all specific content. They do this to avoid the objection that knowledge might come from the specific example rather than the syllogistic form. According to Ibn Taymiyya, this practice empties the syllogism of its intended purpose.
Pay attention to why the logicians abstract: they want the form alone to carry the proof.
2
Understand Ibn Taymiyya's objection
He argues that the goal of knowledge is to know particular things. Abstract forms give no knowledge of particulars; they only show a relation between empty variables. The logicians' method is thus self-defeating because it removes the very content that yields knowledge.
Notice the epistemological shift: Ibn Taymiyya prioritizes knowledge of concrete realities over formal abstraction.
3
Follow his argument against the necessity of burhān
Ibn Taymiyya claims that in rational matters, people often know specific truths without knowing the universal premise. For example, one can see that a particular object exists without needing the universal 'everything that begins to exist has a cause.' Therefore, knowledge is not dependent on universal propositions or syllogistic demonstration.
This is a direct challenge to the foundational claim of Aristotelian epistemology.
4
Assess the implication for prophetic knowledge
He then extends the argument to revealed matters: prophetic knowledge (e.g., rulings, truths about the unseen) is not only independent of burhān but superior to it. The claim that 'no knowledge except through burhān' is false in both rational and revealed sciences.
This final move shows that the critique is not just methodological but also theological.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التمثيل بصور مجردة عن المواد المعينة والمنطقيون يمثلون بصور مجردة عن المواد لا تدل على شيء بعينه لئلا يستفاد العلم بالمثال من صورته المعينة كما يقولون كل ا ب وكل ب ج فكل ا ج لكن المقصود هو العلم المطلوب من المواد المعينة فإذا جردت يظن الظان أن هذا يحتاج إليه في المعينات وليس الأمر كذلك بل إذا طولبوا بالعلم بالمقدمتين الكليتين في جميع مطالبهم العقلية التي لم تؤخذ عن المعصومين تجدهم يحتجون بما يمكن معه العلم بالمعينات المطلوبة بدون العلم بالقضية الكلية فلا يكون العلم بها موقوفاً على البرهان الكلية القضايا النبوية فالقضايا النبوية لا تحتاج إلى القياس العقلي الذي سموه برهان وما يستفاد عن القياس بالعقل من العلوم أيضاً لا يحتاج إلى قياسهم البرهاني فلا يحتاج إليه لا في العقليات ولا في السمعيات فامتنع أن يقال لا يحصل علم إلا بالقياس البرهاني الذي ذكروه
Representation using forms abstracted from specific materials: The logicians represent using forms abstracted from materials that do not indicate anything in particular, so that knowledge of the example is not derived from its specific form, as they say: 'Every A is B, and every B is C, so every A is C.' But the intended goal is the knowledge sought from specific materials. So when they are abstracted, the one who thinks may think that this is needed for specific things, but it is not so. Rather, if they are asked for knowledge of the two universal premises in all their rational demands that are not taken from the infallible, you find them arguing using what allows knowledge of the demanded specific things without knowledge of the universal proposition, so knowledge of them is not dependent on the universal proposition. Prophetic propositions: Prophetic propositions do not need the rational syllogism that they call 'burhan,' and what is derived from syllogism by reason in terms of sciences also does not need their burhan, so neither in rational matters nor in revealed matters does it need it. Thus it is impossible to say that knowledge is only attained through the burhan syllogism that they mentioned.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage contains two interrelated critiques. First, Ibn Taymiyya attacks the logicians' practice of using abstract forms (صور مجردة) like 'Every A is B, every B is C, therefore every A is C.' He argues that such forms are empty of specific content and do not yield knowledge of particular things. The logicians empty the example of specific matter to avoid the objection that knowledge comes from the specific content, but that defeats the purpose because the goal is knowledge of specific things. Second, he argues that both rational knowledge (عقليات) and revealed knowledge (سمعيات) do not require the logicians' burhan. In rational matters, people often know specific things without the universal premise, as in sensory evidence or reports from the infallible (i.e., prophets). Thus the claim that all knowledge depends on burhan is false.

Key Terms
صور مجردة (ṣuwar mujarrada): Abstract forms from which all specific material content has been removed, used by logicians to illustrate syllogistic figures without reference to concrete subjects. [Note: In later logic, 'مجرّد' often refers to abstract concepts, but here it specifically means stripping the example of concrete referents.]
برهان (burhān): The demonstrative syllogism that yields certain knowledge according to the logicians. Ibn Taymiyya uses it critically. [Note: In Aristotle and the Peripatetics, burhan is the highest form of demonstration; Ibn Taymiyya denies its necessity and validity.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) To illustrate the validity of syllogistic figures
B) To ensure knowledge comes from the form not the matter
C) To make the example easier to understand
D) To avoid showing that specific matter is irrelevant

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya exposes the emptiness of abstract syllogistic forms and asserts that both rational and divine knowledge are independent of the logicians' burhān.
