Analogical vs Inclusive Syllogism: Ibn Taymiyya's Defense
Comparison of syllogism types with abstract variables

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare the analogical and inclusive syllogisms and identify the shared middle term in both.

Core Idea
Analogical syllogism shares the same middle term as inclusive syllogism, making it equally valid.

Ibn Taymiyya uses abstract variables (Alif, Ba, Jim, Dal) to demonstrate that analogical syllogism is not weaker than the inclusive categorical syllogism. Both rely on a common middle term. He argues that the inclusion of an example in analogy does not diminish its logical force.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two syllogism types
Find where Ibn Taymiyya names qiyas al-tamthil and qiyas al-shumul. Note that he uses variables to represent terms.
2
Extract the inclusive syllogism structure
The inclusive syllogism is presented as: every Alif is Ba, every Ba is Jim → every Alif is Jim. This is Barbara mood.
Middle term = Ba
3
Extract the analogical syllogism structure
The analogical form: Alif is Jim by analogy to Dal, because Dal is Jim (since it is Ba) and Alif is also Ba.
Dal is the example
4
Compare the middle terms
Both syllogisms use Ba as the middle term. Ibn Taymiyya says the analogical form is 'correct in meaning' despite the extra example.
Thus analogy is not inferior
5
Recognize the epistemological implication
Ibn Taymiyya's point is that rational people use analogy, and it can be more evident (abyan) than the inclusive form.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثالث - الموازنة بين قياس التمثيل» و«قياس الشمول يحصل بالمثال قياس التمثيل، بل قد يكون التمثيل أبين. ولهذا كان العقلاء يقيسون به وكذلك قولهم في «الحدّ» : «إنه لا يحصل بالمثال»، إنما ذلك في المثال قولهم في الحد : إنه لا الذي لا يحصل به التمييز بين المحدود وغيره بحيث يعرف به ما يلازم المحدود طرداً وعكساً، بحيث يوجد حيث وجد وينتفي حيث انتفى. فإن الحدّ المميز للمحدود هو ما به يعرف الملازم المطابق طرداً وعكساً. فكل ما حصل هذا فقد ميّز المحدود من غيره . الحد عند جماهير النظار، ولا يسوّغون إدخال الجنس العام في الحدّ . فإذا كان المقصود الحد بحسب الاسم، فسأل بعض العجم عن مسمى «الخبز»، فاري رغيفاً وقيل له : «هذا»، فقد يفهم أن هذا اللفظ يوجد فيه كل ما هو «خبز» ـ سواء كان على صورة «الرغيف» أو غير صورته. وقد بسط الكلام على ما ذكروه وذكره المنطقيون : في الكلام على «المحصل» وغير ذلك . وهذا هو وجد هذا في الأمثلة المجرّدة إذا كان المقصود إثبات الجيم للألف، والحدّ الأوسط هو الباء فقيل : كل ألف باء وكل باء جيم أنتج : كل ألف جيم ولكن يحتاج ذلك إلى إثبات القضية الكبرى» مع «الصغرى». فإذا قيل : الألف جيم قياساً على الدال لأن الدال هي جيم، وإنما كانت جيماً لأنها باء والألف أيضاً باء، فتكون الألف جيماً لاشتراكهما في المستلزم للجيم، وهو الباء كان هذا صحيحاً في معنى الأول. لكن فيه زيادة مثال قيست عليه الألف، مع أن الحد الأوسط - وهو الباء - موجود فيهما.
The third station: comparison between the analogical syllogism and the inclusive syllogism. The analogical syllogism is obtained by example; indeed analogy may be clearer. Therefore rational people use it in reasoning. Similarly, their statement about definition: 'It is not obtained by example' — that is regarding the example they say in definition: it is that which does not give distinction between the defined and other, such that one knows by it what accompanies the defined both positively and negatively, existing where it exists and ceasing where it ceases. For the distinguishing definition of the defined is that by which one knows the consistent concomitant both positively and negatively. So whatever achieves this has distinguished the defined from others. Definition according to the majority of theoreticians, and they do not permit the inclusion of the general genus in the definition. If the intended definition is according to the name, and some non-Arab asked about the meaning of 'bread', and he was shown a loaf and told: 'This', he might understand that this word includes everything that is 'bread' — whether in the form of a loaf or other forms. And the discussion on what they mentioned and what the logicians mentioned has been elaborated: regarding the discussion on 'al-muhassal' and others. This is found in the abstract examples when the aim is to prove Jim for Alif, and the middle term is Ba, so it is said: Every Alif is Ba, and every Ba is Jim, so it yields: every Alif is Jim. But that requires establishing the major premise along with the minor. So if it is said: Alif is Jim by analogy to Dal, because Dal is Jim, and it is only Jim because it is Ba, and Alif is also Ba, then Alif becomes Jim due to their sharing in what necessitates Jim, which is Ba. This is correct in the sense of the first (syllogism). But it includes an additional example to which Alif is compared, while the middle term — which is Ba — is present in both.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage is Ibn Taymiyya's critical comparison between two types of syllogism: analogical syllogism (qiyas al-tamthil) and inclusive syllogism (qiyas al-shumul or qiyas iqtirani). He begins by stating that the analogical syllogism is obtained through an example, and indeed analogy can be more evident (abyan). Rational people use it in reasoning. He then addresses the logicians' claim that definition (hadd) cannot be obtained by example. Ibn Taymiyya clarifies that their statement refers to a definition that does not provide distinction between the defined and others — i.e., a definition that does not give the concomitant both positively and negatively (ṭardan wa ʿaksan). A proper distinguishing definition is one that gives the concomitant that matches both in occurrence and cessation. He then gives an example: if someone asks the meaning of 'bread' and is shown a loaf, he may understand that the word applies to all forms of bread. This is a kind of teaching by example. Then he shifts to a syllogistic example using the variables Alif, Ba, Jim, Dal to illustrate the difference between the two syllogisms. The inclusive syllogism (every Alif is Ba, every Ba is Jim → every Alif is Jim) requires establishing both premises. The analogical syllogism (Alif is Jim by analogy to Dal) adds an example (Dal) which shares the middle term Ba with Alif. Ibn Taymiyya acknowledges that this is correct in meaning, but the analogical form includes an extra example. This critique shows that the logicians' preference for the inclusive syllogism over analogy is not based on epistemic superiority, as both ultimately rely on the same middle term.

قياس الشمول

B) The same middle term
C) The same example
D) The same major premise

Key Takeaway
Analogical syllogism is not inferior to inclusive syllogism; both rely on a shared middle term, making them logically equivalent.

?
Construct an analogical syllogism to argue that smoking is forbidden using the example of khamr (wine). Identify the shared middle term ('illah).

Teaching Definitions by Example: Ibn Taymiyya's Refutation
Critique of logicians' denial that definition can be taught by example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how examples can effectively teach definitions according to Ibn Taymiyya.

Core Idea
Examples can convey a definition by indicating the essential concomitant, not just the genus and differentia.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges the logicians' claim that definition (hadd) cannot be obtained by example. He argues that a proper definition distinguishes the defined from others by its concomitant (mulazim), and an example can effectively teach that concomitant. He illustrates with the story of a non-Arab learning the meaning of 'bread' by being shown a loaf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim about definition
The logicians say definition cannot be obtained by example. Ibn Taymiyya quotes them: 'إنه لا يحصل بالمثال' (it is not obtained by example).
2
Understand Ibn Taymiyya's counter-argument
He clarifies that what they mean is a definition that does not give distinction (tamyiz) between the defined and others. A proper definition gives the concomitant both positively and negatively.
The concomitant must be present whenever the defined is present, and absent whenever it is absent.
3
Examine the bread example
If a non-Arab asks about 'bread' and is shown a loaf, he may understand that the word applies to all forms of bread. This shows that an example can convey the meaning.
The example (loaf) is an instance of the defined.
4
Connect to the logical principle
Ibn Taymiyya implies that an example can give the essential concomitant: the loaf 'accompanies' bread wherever it is found. So the example teaches the definition.
Thus definition by example is possible when the example is representative.
5
Contrast with the logicians' position
Ibn Taymiyya notes that the logicians require genus and differentia, but he argues that whatever achieves distinction (tamyiz) is a valid definition, including examples.
This is part of his broader critique of Greek logical formalism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Why Demonstration Fails for Possible Beings
Ibn Taymiyya argues that demonstration fails for possible beings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that syllogistic demonstration does not yield universal necessary truths about possible beings.

Core Idea
Demonstration cannot give necessary truths about possible beings because they cannot be eternal with their cause.

The logicians claim that demonstration (burhan) yields universal necessary truths. Ibn Taymiyya challenges this, arguing that the contingent nature of possible beings (mumkinat) makes such necessity impossible. He uses the example of motion and efficient causality to show that a possible effect cannot be eternal with its cause. This lesson unpacks his critique.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target claim
What do the logicians assert about demonstration? They claim it yields universal necessary truths that persist. Find where Ibn Taymiyya states this is false.
Look at the opening: 'المقام الثالث - الوجه السادس : البرهان لا يفيد أموراً كلية واجبة البقاء'
2
Extract Ibn Taymiyya's counter
He says demonstration does not yield such truths. His argument: a possible being (mumkin) by definition can either exist or not; it depends on an efficient cause. The effect cannot be simultaneous with its cause in a particular instance, only the type can be eternal.
Consider the motion example: 'فَقِدَم نوع الفعل كقدم نوع الحركة' – the type is eternal, but each part is temporal.
3
Analyze the consensus argument
Ibn Taymiyya notes that the majority of rational people, including Aristotle, agree that a possible effect cannot be eternal with its cause. Ibn Sina's view that it can is an innovation.
He mentions Ibn Rushd's rejection of Ibn Sina's position.
4
Identify the logical flaw in Ibn Sina's claim
Ibn Sina claimed the celestial sphere is a possible being yet eternal. Ibn Taymiyya argues this is contradictory because an eternal thing cannot accept non-existence, but a possible being does accept it.
The contradiction undermines the claim that demonstration can prove necessary truths about such beings.
5
Conclude the lesson
Thus, demonstration fails for possible beings because their contingent nature prevents necessary universal conclusions about individuals.
This is the first part of Ibn Taymiyya's wider critique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثالث - الوجه السادس : البرهان لا يفيد أموراً كلية واجبة البقاء. وأما «الفاعل» فيمتنع أن يقارنه مفعوله المعيَّن، وإن لم يمتنع أن يكون فاعلاً لشيء بعد شيء. فقِدَم نوع الفعل كقدم نوع الحركة وذلك لا ينافي حدوث كل جزء من أجزائها، بل يستلزمه لامتناع قدم شيء منها بعينه . وهذا مما عليه جماهير العقلاء من جميع الأمم ، حتى أرسطو وأتباعه. فإنهم، الأول ليس مبدعاً للعالم وإن قالوا بقدم العالَم، فهم لم يثبتوا له «مُبدِعاً» ولا «علة فاعلة»، بل «علة غائية يتحرك الفلك للتشبه بها، لأن حركة الفلك إرادية . وهذا القول ـ وهو إن الأوّل ليس مبدعاً للعالم، وإنما هو علة غائية للتشبه به ـ قول ابن سينا وإن كان في غاية الجهل والكفر، فالمقصود أنهم وافقوا سائر العقلاء في أن الممكن المعلول لا يكون قديماً بقدم علّته، كما يقول ذلك ابن سينا وموافقوه . ولهذا أنكر هذا القولَ ابنُ رُشد وأمثاله من الفلاسفة الذين اتبعوا طريقة أرسطو وسائر العقلاء في ذلك، وبينوا أن ما ذكره ابن سينا مما خالف به سلفه وجماهير العقلاء. وكان قصده أن يركب مذهباً من مذهب المتكلمين ومذهب سلفه، فيجعل الموجود الممكن معلول الواجب - مفعولاً له ـ مع كونه أزلياً قديماً بقدمه. واتبعه على إمكان ذلك أتباعه في ذلك كالسُّهْرَوَرْدِي الحلبي ، والرَّازي، والآمدي ، والطُّوسي ، وغيرهم. وزعم الرازي ما ذكره في مُحَصَّله أن القول بـ كون الممكن المفعول المعلول يكون قديماً للموجب بالذات مما اتفق عليه الفلاسفة والمتكلمون، لكن المتكلمون يقولون بالحدوث، لكون الفاعل عندهم فاعلاً بالاختيار . وهذا غلط على الطائفتين بل لم يقل ذلك أحد لا من المتكلمين ولا من الفلاسفة المتقدمين الذين نُقلت إلينا أقوالهم كأرسطو وأمثاله. وإنما قاله ابن سينا وأمثاله . والمتكلمون إذا قالوا بقدم ما يقوم بالربّ من الصفات ونحوها، فلا يقولون إنها مفعولة، ولا معلولة لعلة فاعلة بل الذاتُ القديمة هي الموصوفة بتلك «الصفات» عندهم. فصفاتها من لوازمها، يمتنع تحقق كون «الواجب» واجباً قديماً إلا بـ «صفاته» اللازمة له، كما قد بسط في موضعه. ويمتنع عندهم قدمُ ممكن يقبل الوجود والعدم مع قطع النظر عن فاعله . وكذلك أساطين الفلاسفة يمتنع عندهم «قديم» يقبل العدم، ويمتنع أن يكون الممكن لم يزل واجباً، سواءً قيل إنه واجب بنفسه أو بغيره . ولكن ما ذكره ابن سينا وأمثاله في أن الممكن قد يكون قديماً واجباً بغيره أزلياً أبدياً ، كما يقولونه في «الفلك»، هو الذي فتح عليهم في «الإمكان من الأسئلة القادحة في قولهم ما لا يمكنهم أن يجيبوا عنه كما قد بسط في موضعه. فإن هذا ليس موضع تقرير هذا . ولكن نبهنا به على أن برهانهم القياسي لا يفيد أموراً كلية واجبة البقاء في «الممكنات». وأما واجب الوجود تبارك وتعالى فَـ «القياس» الذي يدعونه لا يدل على ما يختص به، وإنما يدل على أمر مشترك كلي بينه وبين غيره، إذ كان مدلول «القياس» أمراً كلياً.
The third station, the sixth aspect: Demonstration does not yield universal matters that necessarily persist. As for the agent, it is impossible for its specific effect to be simultaneous with it, although it is not impossible for it to be an agent for something after something. So the eternity of the type of act is like the eternity of the type of motion, and that does not contradict the temporal origination of every individual part of it; rather it necessitates it, because the eternity of any individual part of it is impossible. This is a point on which the majority of rational people from all nations agree, even Aristotle and his followers. For they—the First is not the creator of the world, even if they said the world is eternal—they did not posit for Him a 'creator' nor an 'efficient cause', but rather a 'final cause' towards which the celestial sphere moves in order to resemble it, because its motion is voluntary. And this statement—that the First is not the creator of the world, but is only a final cause for resemblance to Him—is the statement of Ibn Sina, even if it is extreme ignorance and disbelief. The point is that they agreed with the rest of the rational people that the possible effect cannot be eternal with the eternity of its cause, as Ibn Sina and his followers claim. For this reason, Ibn Rushd and similar philosophers, who followed the method of Aristotle and the rest of the rational people in that, rejected this statement, and showed that what Ibn Sina mentioned is something in which he contradicted his predecessors and the majority of rational people. His aim was to combine the school of the theologians and the school of his predecessors, making the possible existent an effect of the Necessary—acted upon by Him—while being eternal, pre-eternal with His eternity. He was followed in the possibility of that by his followers in that, such as al-Suhrawardi al-Halabi, al-Razi, al-Amidi, al-Tusi, and others. And al-Razi claimed in his 'Muhassal' that the statement that the possible thing, the acted-upon, the effect, can be eternal for the cause that necessitates by essence is something agreed upon by the philosophers and the theologians, but the theologians say that it occurs in time, because the agent is, in their view, an agent by choice. This is an error against both groups; indeed no one said that, neither among the theologians nor among the earlier philosophers whose statements have been transmitted to us, like Aristotle and his likes. Only Ibn Sina and his likes said it. And the theologians, if they speak of the eternity of the attributes that subsist in the Lord and the like, they do not say that they are effects or caused by an efficient cause; rather, the eternal essence is described by those 'attributes' according to them. So His attributes are among His necessities; it is impossible for the 'Necessary' to be realized as necessary and eternal except through His 'attributes' that are necessary for Him, as has been expounded in its place. And according to them, it is impossible for a possible thing that accepts existence and non-existence, disregarding its agent, to be eternal. Likewise, the leading philosophers hold that it is impossible for an eternal thing to accept non-existence, and it is impossible for the possible thing to have always been necessary, whether it is said to be necessary by itself or by another. Rather, what Ibn Sina and his likes mentioned—that the possible can be eternal, necessary through another, everlasting from eternity to eternity, as they say about the 'celestial sphere'—is what opened against them, concerning 'possibility', the decisive objections against their statement to which they cannot answer, as has been expanded in its place. For this is not the place to settle this. Rather, we have alerted by this that their syllogistic demonstration does not yield universal matters that necessarily persist in 'possible things'. As for the Necessary Being, Blessed and Exalted, the 'syllogism' they claim does not indicate what is specific to Him; rather it indicates a common universal matter between Him and other than Him, since the meaning of the 'syllogism' is a universal matter.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument by Ibn Taymiyya against the claim that syllogistic demonstration yields universal necessary truths. He begins by stating the sixth aspect of the third station: 'Demonstration does not yield universal matters that necessarily persist.' He then discusses the impossibility of a specific effect being simultaneous with its agent, using the example of motion—the type of motion is eternal but each individual part occurs in time. He notes that the majority of rational people agree that a possible effect cannot be eternal with the eternity of its cause. Ibn Taymiyya then critiques Ibn Sina's view that the First is only a final cause, not an efficient cause, and that the celestial sphere moves voluntarily to resemble Him. Ibn Rushd and others rejected this. Ibn Taymiyya explains that Ibn Sina attempted to combine the theologians' view of creation with the philosophers' view of eternity, making the possible effect eternal with its cause. Al-Razi mistakenly claimed that both philosophers and theologians agreed on the possibility of an eternal effect, differing only on the role of choice. Ibn Taymiyya refutes this, stating that neither group held that view; theologians deny the eternity of any possible being, and earlier philosophers also deny that an eternal thing can accept non-existence. The source of confusion is Ibn Sina's claim about the celestial sphere being eternal yet possible. Ibn Taymiyya concludes that syllogistic demonstration cannot yield necessary universal truths about possible beings, and for the Necessary Being, it only indicates common attributes, not what is unique to Him. Thus, the syllogism fails to prove the specific attributes of God.

Key Terms
برهان
ممكن
علة فاعلة
قدم
قياس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, what is wrong with the claim that a possible being can be eternal with its cause?
A) The possible being does not exist.
B) The possible being cannot be eternal because it accepts non-existence.
C) The possible being is necessary by itself.
D) The possible being is not caused.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya argues that syllogistic demonstration cannot yield universal necessary truths about possible beings because their nature is contingent, not necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the key premise in Ibn Taymiyya's argument about the relationship between an efficient cause and its effect. Explain how this premise undermines the claim that demonstration yields necessary truths about possible beings.
?
Consider the statement: 'The world is a possible being, and it is eternal.' Using Ibn Taymiyya's reasoning, explain why a theologian would reject this statement.

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
The Syllogism Cannot Capture God's Unique Attributes
Ibn Taymiyya argues that syllogism fails to indicate God's specific attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Taymiyya's claim that syllogistic demonstration only yields common predicates, not the unique attributes of the Necessary Being.

Core Idea
Syllogism only shows what God shares with creation, not what makes Him unique.

Even if syllogistic demonstration worked for possible beings, Ibn Taymiyya argues it would still fail for the Necessary Being (Wajib al-Wujud). The syllogism's conclusion is always a universal matter, so it can only indicate attributes that God shares with others, not what is specific to Him. This lesson examines this key limitation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target claim about God
Logicians claim that syllogistic demonstration can prove attributes of the Necessary Being. Ibn Taymiyya counters that the syllogism only indicates a common universal matter.
Find the sentence: 'وأما واجب الوجود تبارك وتعالى فَـ «القياس» الذي يدعونه لا يدل على ما يختص به'
2
Analyze why syllogism yields only common predicates
The middle term in a syllogism is a universal concept; the conclusion is also universal. Therefore, any attribute proven about God via syllogism would be a universal that applies to other beings as well.
Ibn Taymiyya says: 'إذ كان مدلول «القياس» أمراً كلياً'
3
Contrast with the theologians' method
Theologians affirm God's attributes as necessary concomitants of His essence, not derived from syllogisms. They do not claim these attributes are caused or proven by a universal premise.
He mentions that attributes are 'من لوازمها' (necessary entailments of the essence).
4
Implication for the scope of demonstration
Thus, syllogism cannot establish God's unique perfections (like His specific knowledge or power). It can only show that He shares some common property with other beings.
This limits the usefulness of syllogistic theology.
5
Conclude the lesson
Ibn Taymiyya's critique undermines the claim that syllogism is the highest tool for knowing God. Theologians must rely on other methods.
This is a direct challenge to the primacy of Greek logic in theology.

The Syllogism

The Three Types of Evidence in Islamic Logic
Author presents the logicians' classification of evidence into syllogism, induction, and analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any instance of inference into syllogism, induction, or analogy based on the direction of inference between universal and particular.

Core Idea
Evidence is divided by inference direction: universal-to-particular (syllogism), particular-to-universal (induction), or particular-to-particular (analogy).

Islamic logicians, following the Aristotelian tradition, classified all evidence (dalil) into three species based on the logical direction of inference. This classification is not just a dry taxonomy—it is the key to understanding how different Islamic sciences construct proofs. In this lesson, you will learn to parse this classification and apply it to real examples from usul al-fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the genus and species
The passage states that the logicians divided the genus 'evidence' into three species: syllogism, induction, and analogy. Note that 'evidence' here means any proof that leads to knowledge of a conclusion.
The genus is الدليل, the species are القياس, الاستقراء, التمثيل.
2
Understand the basis of division
The division is based on the direction of inference between the universal (كلي) and the particular (جزئي). The logicians expressed this in terms of 'general' (عام) and 'specific' (خاص). There are three possible directions: from universal to particular, from particular to universal, or from one particular to another.
Direction is the differentia that distinguishes each species.
3
Map each direction to the type name
Universal-to-particular is syllogism (قياس الشمول). Particular-to-universal is induction (استقراء). One particular to another is analogy (تمثيل).
Syllogism here is specifically the categorical syllogism that infers from a universal rule to a specific case.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Take the kalam argument: 'Every contingent thing has a cause; the world is contingent; therefore the world has a cause.' This is a universal-to-particular inference (syllogism). Now consider: 'Wine is intoxicating and prohibited; beer is intoxicating, so beer is prohibited.' That is a particular-to-particular inference (analogy).
Make sure you can explain why each example fits its category.

فصل
أقوال المنطقيين في «الدليل» «والقياس»
وأيضاً فإنهم قسموا جنس «الدليل» إلى «القياس» و «الاستقراء» و «التمثيل».
والاستقراء» قالوا: لأن الاستدلال إما أن يكون بـ «الكلي» على «الجزئي)»، أو بـ «الجزئي» على «الكلي»، أو بأحد «الجزئيين» على الآخر. وربما عبروا عن ذلك بـ «الخاص» و «العام»، فقالوا: إما أن يستدل بـ «العام» على «الخاص» ، أو بـ «الخاص» على (العام)» ، أو بأحد «الخاصَّين على الآخر .
قياس الشمول قالوا: والأول هو «القياس»، يعنون به قياس الشمول»، فإنهم يخصونه باسم
Chapter: Statements of the logicians regarding 'evidence' and 'syllogism'. And also, they divided the genus 'evidence' into 'syllogism', 'induction', and 'analogy'. And 'induction'? They said: because inference is either by the universal on the particular, or by the particular on the universal, or by one of the two particulars on the other. And sometimes they expressed that by 'the specific' and 'the general', so they said: either one infers by the general on the specific, or by the specific on the general, or by one of the two specifics on the other. Syllogism of inclusion: They said: the first is 'syllogism', meaning by it the syllogism of inclusion, for they specify it by the name
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage is a definitional classification of the types of evidence (dalin) according to the logicians. The author explains that the genus of evidence is divided into three species: syllogism (qiyas), induction (istiqra'), and analogy (tamthil). The basis of classification is the direction of inference between the universal (kulli) and the particular (juz'i). If the inference is from the universal to the particular, it is called qiyas, specifically qiyas shumul (syllogism of inclusion). If from the particular to the universal, it is induction. If from one particular to another particular, it is analogy. The logicians also express this in terms of 'general' (amm) and 'specific' (khass). For example, inferring that 'every intoxicant is forbidden' (general) applies to 'nabidh' (specific) is a syllogism. Inferring from observing that wine and beer are forbidden to the general rule that all intoxicants are forbidden is induction. Inferring that nabidh is forbidden because it shares the property of intoxication with wine is analogy. This classification is foundational for understanding the types of argumentation in Islamic logic.

Key Terms
الدليل
القياس
الاستقراء
التمثيل
قياس الشمول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the logicians, what type of inference is a syllogism?
A) From particular to universal
B) From universal to particular
C) From one particular to another
D) From cause to effect

Key Takeaway
The logicians classify evidence into three types based on the direction of inference: universal-to-particular (syllogism), particular-to-universal (induction), and particular-to-particular (analogy).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Consider the statement: 'Every intoxicant is unlawful; khamr is an intoxicant; therefore khamr is unlawful.' Identify the type of inference and explain the direction of inference (universal/particular).
?
2. Provide your own example for each of the three types of evidence using an Islamic science topic (e.g., rulings on prayer, fasting, or theological proofs).

The Syllogism

Division of Syllogism: Categorical vs. Conditional
Division of qiyas into categorical and conditional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish categorical (iqtirani) from conditional (istithna'i) syllogisms by identifying whether the conclusion is stated actually or potentially.

Core Idea
A categorical syllogism holds conclusion potentially; conditional syllogism holds it actually.

Muslim logicians classified syllogisms into two main types: categorical (iqtirani) and conditional (istithna'i). The difference lies in how the conclusion is present. In a conditional syllogism, the conclusion or its contradictory is explicitly mentioned in the premises. In a categorical syllogism, it is only implied and must be extracted. This lesson unpacks this fundamental division using examples from Islamic law and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main types of qiyas
Find where the logicians divide qiyas into 'al-iqtirani' and 'al-istithna'i'. Note the Arabic terms.
2
Understand the defining criterion
The text says the conditional syllogism has the conclusion or its contradictory 'actually mentioned' (madhkūratan fīhi bil-fiʿl), while the categorical has it 'potentially' (bil-quwwa).
3
Analyze the example of a categorical syllogism
The text gives: 'Every nabidh is intoxicating, and every intoxicating thing is forbidden.' Identify that the conclusion 'nabidh is forbidden' is not explicitly stated but follows from the premises.
This example uses Islamic legal categories (nabidh, intoxicants).
4
Compare with the conditional example
The conditional syllogism example is: 'If the prayer is valid, then the worshipper is purified.' Here, the conclusion (e.g., 'the prayer is valid') is part of the conditional statement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قالوا: «القياس» ينقسم إلى «الاقتراني» و «الاستثنائي». فـ «الاستثنائي» ما تكون النتيجة أو نقيضها مذكورةً فيه بالفعل، و «الاقتراني» ما تكون فيه بالقوة، كالمؤلف من القضايا «الحملية»، كقولنا: كل نبيذ مسكر، وكل مسكر حرام.
و «الاستثنائي» ما يؤلف من «الشرطيات»، وهو نوعان:
أحدهما: «المتصلة»، كقولنا: إن كانت الصلاة صحيحة فالمصلي متطهر. واستثناء عين المقدم ينتج عين التالي، واستثناء نقيض التالي ينتج نقيض المقدم.
والثاني: «المنفصلة». وهي إما مانعة الجمع والخلو، كقولنا: العدد إما زوج وإما فرد، فإن هذين لا يجتمعان، ولا يخلو العدد عن أحدهما. وإما مانعة الجمع فقط، كقولنا: هذا إما أبيض وإما أسود، أي لا يجتمع السواد والبياض، وقد يخلو المحل عنهما. وإما مانعة الخلو، فهي التي يمتنع فيها عدم الجزئين جميعاً، ولا يمتنع اجتماعهما.
وقد يقولون: مانعة الجمع والخلو هي الشرطية الحقيقية، وهي مطابقة مانعة الخلو للنقيضين في العموم والخصوص. ومانعة الجمع هي أخص من النقيضين، فإن الضدين لا يجتمعان وقد يرتفعان وهما أخص من النقيضين.
وأما مانعة الخلو فإنها أعم من النقيضين. وقد يصعب عليهم تمثيل ذلك بخلاف النوعين الأولين فإن أمثالهما كثيرة. ويمثلونه بقول القائل: «هذا راكب البحر، أو لا يغرق فيه»، أي لا يخلو منهما، فإنه لا يغرق إلا إذا كان في البحر. فإما أن لا يغرق...
Then they said: 'The syllogism divides into the categorical and the conditional. The conditional is that in which the conclusion or its contradictory is actually mentioned, and the categorical is that in which it is potential, like that composed of categorical propositions, as we say: Every nabidh is intoxicating, and every intoxicating thing is forbidden. And the conditional is composed of conditional propositions, and it is of two types: One is the conjunctive, as we say: If the prayer is valid, then the worshipper is purified. Affirming the exact antecedent yields the exact consequent, and affirming the contradictory of the consequent yields the contradictory of the antecedent. The second is the disjunctive. It is either of the type preventing both collection and emptiness, as we say: The number is either even or odd, for these two are not collected, and the number is not empty of one of them. Or it is of the type preventing collection only, as we say: This is either white or black, meaning that whiteness and blackness are not collected, and the subject may be empty of both. Or it is of the type preventing emptiness, which is that in which the non-existence of both parts is impossible, but their coexistence is not impossible. And they sometimes say: The type preventing both collection and emptiness is the true conditional, and it matches the type preventing emptiness with respect to the two contradictories in generality and specificity. The type preventing collection is more specific than the two contradictories, for contraries do not collect and may both be absent, so they are more specific than the two contradictories. As for the type preventing emptiness, it is more general than the two contradictories. It may be difficult for them to exemplify that, unlike the first two types, for their examples are many. And they exemplify it by the saying of someone: 'This one is riding the sea, or he will not drown in it,' meaning that he is not empty of them, for he only drowns if he is in the sea. So either he will not drown...
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage reports the logicians' further division of qiyas into categorical (iqtirani) and conditional (istithna'i). The categorical syllogism is composed of categorical premises (hamli) and contains the conclusion potentially; the conditional syllogism is composed of conditional propositions (shartiyyat) and contains the conclusion or its contradictory actually. The conditional is then divided into conjunctive (muttasila) and disjunctive (munfasila). For the conjunctive, the example is: 'If the prayer is valid, then the worshipper is purified.' Two valid moods are given: affirming the antecedent (modus ponens) yields the consequent, and denying the consequent (modus tollens) yields the negation of the antecedent. The disjunctive is further divided into three types: (1) man'at al-jam' wa al-khulu (preventing both collection and emptiness), exemplified by 'The number is either even or odd'—both cannot be true and one must be true; (2) man'at al-jam' (preventing collection only), exemplified by 'This is either white or black'—both cannot be true but both may be false; (3) man'at al-khulu (preventing emptiness only), where both cannot be false but both may be true, exemplified by 'This one is riding the sea or he will not drown'—he cannot be neither riding nor not drowning, but he could be both (riding and drowning). The text notes that the first type is the 'true conditional' and compares the logical relations to the two contradictories (naqidayn). This classification is standard in Aristotelian logic as transmitted by Muslim logicians. Ibn Taymiyya presents it here for critical analysis elsewhere.

Key Terms
الاقتراني
الاستثنائي
الحملية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the logicians in this passage, what is the defining characteristic of a conditional syllogism (istithna'i)?
A) It is composed of categorical premises
B) The conclusion or its contradictory is actually mentioned
C) It yields probable conclusion only
D) It must have two premises

Key Takeaway
Categorical syllogisms contain their conclusion potentially; conditional syllogisms contain it actually.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the following as categorical or conditional syllogism and explain your reasoning: 'Every Muslim must pray. Zayd is a Muslim. Therefore Zayd must pray.' And 'If the child is mature, then he is accountable; he is mature; therefore he is accountable.'

Conjunctive Conditional (Muttasila) and Its Valid Moods
Conjunctive conditional syllogism and its valid moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a conjunctive conditional syllogism and apply modus ponens and modus tollens using Islamic examples.

Core Idea
Affirming the antecedent yields the consequent; denying the consequent yields the denial of the antecedent.

The conjunctive conditional (muttasila) is built from 'if...then' propositions. The logicians taught two valid argument forms: affirming the antecedent (modus ponens) and denying the consequent (modus tollens). Using the example from prayer purification, this lesson shows how to apply these rules correctly.

Guided Path
1
Locate the conjunctive conditional definition
Find the sentence describing 'al-muttasila'. It is introduced as one of the two types of conditional syllogisms.
2
Examine the example given
The passage says: 'If the prayer is valid, then the worshipper is purified.' Identify the antecedent (valid prayer) and consequent (purification).
3
Understand the two valid moods
The text states two rules: (1) affirming the antecedent (istithnā' ʿayn al-muqaddam) produces the consequent; (2) denying the consequent (istithnā' naqīḍ al-tālī) produces the denial of the antecedent.
4
Apply the rules to the example
If we affirm 'the prayer is valid', we conclude 'the worshipper is purified' (modus ponens). If we deny 'the worshipper is purified' (i.e., say he is not purified), we conclude 'the prayer is not valid' (modus tollens).

Types of Disjunctive Conditional (Munfasila)
Three types of disjunctive conditional syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of disjunctive conditional: exclusive (mani'at al-jam' wa al-khulu), inclusive (mani'at al-jam'), and exhaustive (mani'at al-khulu), using examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Disjunctives differ in whether both parts can be true or false; only the exclusive type is the true conditional.

Disjunctive conditionals (munfasila) are 'either...or' propositions. Muslim logicians classified them into three types based on the logical relation between the disjuncts. The first is exclusive (both cannot be true and cannot be false), the second inclusive (only cannot be true together), and the third exhaustive (cannot both be false). This lesson uses the examples from the text to clarify the differences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of disjunctive
The passage lists: (1) mani'at al-jam' wa al-khulu, (2) mani'at al-jam', (3) mani'at al-khulu. Locate these terms.
2
Understand the first type with example
The first is like 'The number is either even or odd.' Both cannot be true (a number cannot be both even and odd) and both cannot be false (a number must be one or the other). This is the exclusive disjunction.
3
Understand the second type
The second is like 'This is either white or black.' Both cannot be true (cannot be both colors) but both may be false (the object could be red, etc.). This is inclusive disjunction (or mutually exclusive but not exhaustive).
4
Understand the third type
The third is like 'This one is riding the sea or he will not drown in it.' Both cannot be false (if he is not riding, he will drown? Actually the example is tricky: 'He is either riding the sea or not drowning'—he cannot be neither riding nor not drowning, but he can be both (riding and drowning). This is an exhaustive disjunction where at least one must be true.
The text notes the difficulty in exemplifying this type.
5
Compare the types to the two contradictories (naqidayn)
The passage relates the types to the logical relation of contradiction: mani'at al-jam' wa al-khulu is exactly like contradictories (both cannot be true and both cannot be false). The second is more specific (like contraries), and the third is more general.

This passage reports the logicians' further division of qiyas into categorical (iqtirani) and conditional (istithna'i). The categorical syllogism is composed of categorical premises (hamli) and contains the conclusion potentially; the conditional syllogism is composed of conditional propositions (shartiyyat) and contains the conclusion or its contradictory actually. The conditional is then divided into conjunctive (muttasila) and disjunctive (munfasila). For the conjunctive, the example is: 'If the prayer is valid, then the worshipper is purified.' Two valid moods are given: affirming the antecedent (modus ponens) yields the consequent, and denying the consequent (modus tollens) yields the negation of the antecedent. The disjunctive is further divided into three types: (1) man'at al-jam' wa al-khulu (preventing both collection and emptiness), exemplified by 'The number is either even or odd'—both cannot be true and one must be true; (2) man'at al-jam' (preventing collection only), exemplified by 'This is either white or black'—both cannot be true but both may be false; (3) man'at al-khulu (preventing emptiness only), where both cannot be false but both may be true, exemplified by 'This one is riding the sea or he will not drown'—he cannot be neither riding nor not drowning, but he could be both (riding and drowning). The text notes that the first type is the 'true conditional' and compares the logical relations to the two contradictories (naqidayn). This classification is standard in Aristotelian logic as transmitted by Muslim logicians. Ibn Taymiyya presents it here for critical analysis elsewhere.

Key Terms
المنفصلة
مانعة الجمع والخلو
مانعة الجمع
مانعة الخلو
النقيضين

Key Takeaway
Disjunctive conditionals vary in whether both disjuncts can be true or false; this determines the valid inference rules.

Distinguishing Categorical from Hypothetical Syllogisms
Division of qiyas into categorical and hypothetical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a categorical (iqtirani) and a hypothetical (istithna'i) syllogism based on whether the conclusion is explicitly mentioned, and identify examples from Islamic fiqh.

Core Idea
The conclusion is either explicit or implicit — that is the key difference between the two types of qiyas.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya reports the logicians' division of qiyas into two main types: iqtirani (categorical) where the conclusion is only potentially present, and istithna'i (hypothetical) where the conclusion or its contradictory is actually stated. Understanding this division is the first step in mastering syllogistic reasoning in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
What is being divided? The term 'qiyas' (syllogism) is being classified into two types.
2
Extract the two types
Name the two types: al-qiyas al-iqtirani (categorical) and al-qiyas al-istithna'i (hypothetical).
3
Understand the distinguishing feature
Istithna'i has the conclusion or its contradictory explicitly mentioned (bil-fi'l). Iqtirani has the conclusion only potentially (bil-quwwa) in the premises.
4
Analyze the example
The example: 'Every nabidh is intoxicating, and every intoxicant is forbidden' is iqtirani because the conclusion 'nabidh is forbidden' is not stated but inferred.
This is a standard fiqh argument.
5
Connect to subsequent content
Note that istithna'i syllogisms are composed of conditional propositions (shartiyyat), which will be explored in later lessons.

Source Text

Then they said: 'Syllogism divides into categorical and hypothetical. The hypothetical is that in which the conclusion or its contradictory is actually mentioned, and the categorical is that in which it is potentially present, like one composed of categorical propositions, as our statement: 'Every nabidh is intoxicating, and every intoxicating thing is forbidden.' The hypothetical is composed of conditional propositions. It is two types: one is the connected conditional, like our statement: 'If the prayer is valid, then the praying person is pure.' The exception of the very antecedent produces the very consequent, and the exception of the contradictory of the consequent produces the contradictory of the antecedent. The second is the disjunctive. It is either the preventive of both union and void, like our statement: 'The number is either even or odd' — these two do not unite, and the number is not void of either. Or it is preventive of union only, like our statement: 'This is either white or black' — i.e., blackness and whiteness do not unite, but the subject may be void of both. Or it is preventive of void, which is that in which the non-existence of both parts together is impossible, but their union is not impossible. And they sometimes say: The preventive of both union and void is the real conditional, and it matches the preventive of void with respect to contradictories in generality and particularity. The preventive of union is more specific than contradictories — for the two opposites do not unite and may both be absent, and they are more specific than contradictories. As for the preventive of void, it is more general than contradictories. And it may be difficult for them to give an example of that, unlike the first two types whose examples are many. They give an example like the one who says: 'This is a rider of the sea, or he does not drown in it' — i.e., it is not void of the two, for he does not drown except if he is in the sea. So either he does not drown in it, in which case he is not its rider, or he is its rider, and it is possible to combine riding and drowning. Many examples exist, like our statement: 'This is alive, or not knowing, or powerful, or hearing, or seeing, or speaking' — for if life exists, it is one of the two divisions, and if it is absent, these attributes are absent, and there may be a living being not described by that. Similarly, if it is said: 'This is purified, or not praying' — for if prayer is absent, purification is absent, and if purification exists, that is the other division, so the matter is not void of them. And likewise every absence of a condition and existence of its conditioned. For if the matter is disjunctively posited between the existence of the conditioned and the absence of the condition, that is preventive of void. For the matter is not void of the existence and non-existence of the condition, and when the condition is absent, the conditioned is absent. So the matter becomes not void of the existence of the conditioned and the absence of the condition.'
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of syllogisms as reported by Ibn Taymiyya from the logicians. He first divides qiyas into two main types: iqtirani (categorical) where the conclusion is implicitly contained in the premises, and istithna'i (hypothetical) where the conclusion or its contradictory is explicitly mentioned. He gives a fiqh example: 'Every nabidh is intoxicating, every intoxicant is forbidden' — a categorical syllogism. For hypothetical syllogisms, they are composed of conditional propositions (shartiyyat). Two subtypes: muttasil (connected conditional) like 'If the prayer is valid, then the praying person is pure' — from which it is valid to affirm the antecedent to get the consequent, or deny the consequent to get the denial of the antecedent. The second subtype is munfasil (disjunctive), further divided into three: (1) mani'at al-jam' wa al-khulu (preventive of both union and void), e.g., 'The number is either even or odd' — they cannot both be true nor both false; (2) mani'at al-jam' only (preventive of union), e.g., 'This is either white or black' — cannot both be true but may both be false; (3) mani'at al-khulu (preventive of void), e.g., 'This is a rider of the sea, or he does not drown' — cannot both be false but may both be true. Ibn Taymiyya notes that the preventive of both union and void is the 'real conditional' and corresponds to strict disjunction, while the preventive of void is inclusive disjunction. He then provides examples from Islamic concepts: life and attributes (knowledge, power, etc.) show a relationship where life is a condition, so 'This is alive, or not knowing' is preventive of void because if life is absent, knowledge is absent (cannot have knowledge without life), but life can exist without knowledge. Similarly, purification (taharah) and prayer (salat) — if prayer is absent, purification is absent (since prayer requires purification), but purification may exist without prayer. These examples illustrate the logical relationships using familiar Islamic legal and theological concepts.

Key Terms
القياس الاقتراني
القياس الاستثنائي

The fundamental division of qiyas is based on whether the conclusion is explicitly stated (istithna'i) or only implied (iqtirani).

Connected Conditional (Muttasil) and Its Valid Inferences
Rules for the connected conditional syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a valid modus ponens or modus tollens argument from a connected conditional proposition, using Islamic examples.

Core Idea
In a connected conditional, affirming the antecedent gives the consequent, and denying the consequent gives the denial of the antecedent.

After introducing the hypothetical syllogism, the passage explains the connected conditional (muttasil) and its two valid inference rules. These rules are essential for reasoning with conditional statements in Islamic legal and theological arguments. Understanding them prevents common fallacies like affirming the consequent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the antecedent and consequent
In 'If A then B', A is the antecedent (muqaddam) and B is the consequent (tali). In the example 'If the prayer is valid, then the person is pure', the antecedent is 'prayer is valid' and consequent is 'person is pure'.
2
Understand the rule for antecedent exception
Exception (istithna) of the very antecedent (ayn al-muqaddam) yields the very consequent. So if we affirm the antecedent, we can conclude the consequent.
This is modus ponens.
3
Understand the rule for consequent exception
Exception of the contradictory of the consequent (naqid al-tali) yields the contradictory of the antecedent. So if we deny the consequent, we can deny the antecedent.
This is modus tollens.
4
Practice with an Islamic example
Take: 'If a person performs wudu, then he is pure. He performed wudu. Therefore he is pure.' This is a valid modus ponens.
5
Identify invalid forms
Affirming the consequent ('He is pure, therefore he performed wudu') and denying the antecedent ('He did not perform wudu, therefore he is not pure') are invalid. Note why these break the rule.
Common fallacies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of syllogisms as reported by Ibn Taymiyya from the logicians. He first divides qiyas into two main types: iqtirani (categorical) where the conclusion is implicitly contained in the premises, and istithna'i (hypothetical) where the conclusion or its contradictory is explicitly mentioned. He gives a fiqh example: 'Every nabidh is intoxicating, every intoxicant is forbidden' — a categorical syllogism. Types of Disjunctive Syllogisms (Munfasil)
Classification of disjunctive syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a disjunctive proposition into one of three types: preventive of both union and void, preventive of union only, or preventive of void only, based on the possibilities of truth and falsehood of the disjuncts.

Core Idea
Disjunctions are not all alike — some are exclusive, some are inclusive, and some are exhaustive.

The disjunctive syllogism (munfasil) comes in three varieties, depending on whether the two disjuncts can be both true, both false, or neither. The passage gives clear examples: even/odd (neither both true nor both false), white/black (not both true but can both be false), and rider/drown (can both be true but not both false). Understanding these distinctions is crucial for evaluating arguments in Islamic law and theology.

Guided Path
1
Understand 'jam' and 'khulu'
Jam' (union) means both disjuncts are true together. Khulu (void) means both are false together. Each type prevents one or both of these possibilities.
2
Examine mani'at al-jam' wa al-khulu
This type prevents both jam' and khulu: the disjuncts cannot both be true and cannot both be false. Example: 'The number is even or odd' — a number cannot be both even and odd, nor neither.
This is exclusive disjunction.
3
Examine mani'at al-jam' only
This type prevents jam' only: the disjuncts cannot both be true, but can both be false. Example: 'This is white or black' — it cannot be both white and black, but it could be red (both false).
4
Examine mani'at al-khulu only
This type prevents khulu only: the disjuncts cannot both be false, but can both be true. Example: 'This is a rider of the sea or he does not drown' — it cannot be that he is neither a rider nor not drown (since if he is not a rider, he must not drown? Actually, the example is tricky: both can be true if he rides and drowns.
This is inclusive disjunction (at least one true).
5
Create an Islamic example
Think of 'This act is obligatory or forbidden' — can both be true? No. Can both be false? Yes, if the act is mubah. So this is mani'at al-jam' only.

ثم قالوا : «القياس» ينقسم إلى الاقتراني» و «الاستثنائي». فـ «الاستثنائي» ما تكون النتيجة أو نقيضها مذكورةً فيه بالفعل و الاقتراني ما تكون فيه بالقوة، كالمؤلف من القضايا «الحَمْلية»، كقولنا : كل نبيذ مسكر، وكل مسكر حرام». و الإستثنائي» ما يؤلف من «الشرطيات»، وهو نوعان: أحدهما : متصلة»، كقولنا : إن كانت الصلوة صحيحة فالمصلي متطهر». واستثناء عين المقدَّم يُنتج عين التالي، واستثناء نقيض التالي ينتج نقيض المقدَّم . والثاني: «المنفصلة». وهي إما مانعةُ الجمع والخُلُو»، كقولنا : العدد إما زوج وإما فرد فإن هذين لا يجتمعان ، ولا يخلو «العدد» عن أحدهما. وإما مانعة الجمع» فقط، كقولنا : «هذا إما أبيض وإما أسود أي لا يجتمع السواد والبياض، وقد يخلو المحلُّ عنهما . وإما مانعة الخلو»، فهي التي يمتنع فيها عدم الجزئين جميعاً، ولا يمتنع اجتماعهما . وقد يقولون: مانعة الجمع والخلو هي الشرطية الحقيقية وهي مطابقة مانعة الخلو» للنقيضين في العموم والخصوص . و مانعة الجمع» هي أخص من النقيضين، فإن الضدين لا يجتمعان وقد يرتفعان وهما أخص من النقيضين . وأما مانعة الخلو» فإنها أعم من النقيضين. وقد يصعب عليهم تمثيل ذلك بخلاف النوعين الأولين فإن أمثالهما كثيرة ويمثلونه بقول القائل: «هذا راكب البحر، أو لا يغرق فيه أي لا يخلو منهما فإنه لا يغرق إلا إذا كان في البحر. فإما أن لا يغرق فيه، وحينئذ لا يكون راكبه ؛ وإما أن يكون راكبه ، وقد يجتمع أن يركب ويغرق . والأمثال كثيرة، كقولنا : هذا حي ، أو ليس بعالم، أو قادر، أو سميع، أو بصير، أو متكلم». فإنه إن وجدت الحياة فهو أحد القسمين، وإن عُدمت عدمت هذه الصفات، وقد يكون حيّاً من لا يوصف بذلك. وكذلك إذا قيل: «هذا متطهر، أو ليس بمصلّ»، فإنه إن عُدمت الصلاة عُدمت الطهارة، وإن وُجدت الطهارة فهو القسم الآخر، فلا يخلو الأمر منهما . وكذلك كلُّ عَدَم شرط و وجودِ مشروطه. فإنه إذا رُدّد الأمر بين وجود المشروط وعدم الشرط كان ذلك مانعاً من الخلق. فإنه لا يخلو الأمر من وجود الشرط وعدمه، وإذا عُدم عُدم الشرط. فصار الأمر لا يخلو من وجود المشروط وعدم الشرط.
Then they said: 'Syllogism divides into categorical and hypothetical. The hypothetical is that in which the conclusion or its contradictory is actually mentioned, and the categorical is that in which it is potentially present, like one composed of categorical propositions, as our statement: 'Every nabidh is intoxicating, and every intoxicating thing is forbidden.' The hypothetical is composed of conditional propositions. It is two types: one is the connected conditional, like our statement: 'If the prayer is valid, then the praying person is pure.' The exception of the very antecedent produces the very consequent, and the exception of the contradictory of the consequent produces the contradictory of the antecedent. The second is the disjunctive. It is either the preventive of both union and void, like our statement: 'The number is either even or odd' — these two do not unite, and the number is not void of either. Or it is preventive of union only, like our statement: 'This is either white or black' — i.e., blackness and whiteness do not unite, but the subject may be void of both. Or it is preventive of void, which is that in which the non-existence of both parts together is impossible, but their union is not impossible. And they sometimes say: The preventive of both union and void is the real conditional, and it matches the preventive of void with respect to contradictories in generality and particularity. The preventive of union is more specific than contradictories — for the two opposites do not unite and may both be absent, and they are more specific than contradictories. As for the preventive of void, it is more general than contradictories. And it may be difficult for them to give an example of that, unlike the first two types whose examples are many. They give an example like the one who says: 'This is a rider of the sea, or he does not drown in it' — i.e., it is not void of the two, for he does not drown except if he is in the sea. So either he does not drown in it, in which case he is not its rider, or he is its rider, and it is possible to combine riding and drowning. Many examples exist, like our statement: 'This is alive, or not knowing, or powerful, or hearing, or seeing, or speaking' — for if life exists, it is one of the two divisions, and if it is absent, these attributes are absent, and there may be a living being not described by that. Similarly, if it is said: 'This is purified, or not praying' — for if prayer is absent, purification is absent, and if purification exists, that is the other division, so the matter is not void of them. And likewise every absence of a condition and existence of its conditioned. For if the matter is disjunctively posited between the existence of the conditioned and the absence of the condition, that is preventive of void. For the matter is not void of the existence and non-existence of the condition, and when the condition is absent, the conditioned is absent. So the matter becomes not void of the existence of the conditioned and the absence of the condition.'
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of syllogisms as reported by Ibn Taymiyya from the logicians. He first divides qiyas into two main types: iqtirani (categorical) where the conclusion is implicitly contained in the premises, and istithna'i (hypothetical) where the conclusion or its contradictory is explicitly mentioned. He gives a fiqh example: 'Every nabidh is intoxicating, every intoxicant is forbidden' — a categorical syllogism. For hypothetical syllogisms, they are composed of conditional propositions (shartiyyat). Two subtypes: muttasil (connected conditional) like 'If the prayer is valid, then the praying person is pure' — from which it is valid to affirm the antecedent to get the consequent, or deny the consequent to get the denial of the antecedent. The second subtype is munfasil (disjunctive), further divided into three: (1) mani'at al-jam' wa al-khulu (preventive of both union and void), e.g., 'The number is either even or odd' — they cannot both be true nor both false; (2) mani'at al-jam' only (preventive of union), e.g., 'This is either white or black' — cannot both be true but may both be false; (3) mani'at al-khulu (preventive of void), e.g., 'This is a rider of the sea, or he does not drown' — cannot both be false but may both be true. Ibn Taymiyya notes that the preventive of both union and void is the 'real conditional' and corresponds to strict disjunction, while the preventive of void is inclusive disjunction. He then provides examples from Islamic concepts: life and attributes (knowledge, power, etc.) show a relationship where life is a condition, so 'This is alive, or not knowing' is preventive of void because if life is absent, knowledge is absent (cannot have knowledge without life), but life can exist without knowledge. Similarly, purification (taharah) and prayer (salat) — if prayer is absent, purification is absent (since prayer requires purification), but purification may exist without prayer. These examples illustrate the logical relationships using familiar Islamic legal and theological concepts.

Key Terms
الشرطية المنفصلة
مانعة الجمع والخلو
مانعة الجمع
مانعة الخلو

B) Mani'at al-khulu (preventive of void only)
C) Mani'at al-jam' wa al-khulu (preventive of both union and void)
D) Muttasila (connected conditional)

Key Takeaway
The three types of disjunction differ based on whether the disjuncts can both be true, both be false, or neither.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Given 'This act is either wajib or haram', determine which type of disjunction it is. If the act could be mubah, which type would it become? Explain.
?

Mani'at al-Khulu in Islamic Examples
Examples of mani'at al-khulu from Islamic sciences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the concept of mani'at al-khulu (preventive of void) to real Islamic examples involving conditions and their effects in fiqh and kalam.

Core Idea
In mani'at al-khulu, the two alternatives cannot all be false — one must be true, but both can be true.

Ibn Taymiyya provides several examples from Islamic theology and jurisprudence to illustrate mani'at al-khulu. These include relationships between life and knowledge, purification and prayer, and various conditional connections. Understanding these examples shows how disjunctive reasoning operates in Islamic discourse, especially regarding conditions and their effects.

Guided Path
1
Identify the pattern
Mani'at al-khulu statements often have the form 'X or not Y' where Y is dependent on X. For example, 'This is alive or not knowing' — knowledge requires life, so it cannot be that both are false (dead and knowing).
2
Examine the life and attributes example
The passage says: 'This is alive, or not knowing...' If both false: not alive and knowing — impossible because knowledge requires life. So at least one must be true. However, both can be true if alive but not knowing? Actually 'not knowing' is true if not knowing, but if alive and knowing then 'not knowing' is false, so they are not both true. Wait, the example: 'This is alive, or not knowing' — if alive and not knowing, both true. So it is not impossible for both to be true. Hence mani'at al-khulu.
The condition: life is necessary for knowledge.
3
Examine the purification and prayer example
'This is purified, or not praying' — prayer requires purification. So it cannot be that both are false: not purified and praying. Both can be true if purified but not praying. So mani'at al-khulu.
4
Understand the general principle
The passage generalizes: 'every absence of a condition and existence of its conditioned' forms a mani'at al-khulu. For example, 'The prayer is valid or the person is not pure' (since validity requires purity).
5
Create your own example
Think of a condition in fiqh, e.g., 'This sale is valid or the price is not paid' (since validity requires payment). Test if both can be false (invalid and paid? That is possible if invalid despite payment, so not mani'at al-khulu. Need proper condition.
Not all conditionals produce mani'at al-khulu.

The Syllogism

The Four Figures of Categorical Syllogism
Classification of the four figures based on middle term position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a categorical syllogism into one of the four figures based on the position of the middle term and identify which types of conclusions each figure yields.

Core Idea
The middle term's position determines the figure and the range of conclusions each figure can produce.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya reports the logicians' division of categorical syllogisms into four figures. Understanding these figures is crucial for evaluating whether a given syllogism can yield a certain conclusion. Let's explore each figure and its characteristics.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term
Find the term that appears in both premises. This is the 'middle term' that connects the subject and predicate of the conclusion.
In a valid categorical syllogism, there are exactly three terms: major, minor, and middle.
2
Determine the middle term's position in each premise
Check whether the middle term is the subject or predicate in the major premise and in the minor premise. The figure is defined by this pattern.
Major premise contains the major term (predicate of conclusion); minor premise contains the minor term (subject of conclusion).
3
Match the pattern to one of the four figures
Use the description from the passage: First figure: middle predicate in major, subject in minor. Second: middle predicate in both. Third: middle subject in both. Fourth: middle subject in major, predicate in minor.
The fourth figure is considered 'far from natural' according to the passage.
4
Identify the types of conclusions each figure can produce
First figure yields all four types (universal/particular, affirmative/negative). Second yields only negative conclusions. Third yields only particular conclusions. Fourth yields particulars and universal negation.
This determines which figures are directly useful for proving a given claim.
5
Recognize why immediate inferences are needed
Only the first figure directly yields all conclusion types. To get other types from other figures, logicians use contradictory, conversion, and contrapositive to transform premises.
This motivates the second part of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قسَّموا «الاقتراني» إلى الأشكال الأربعة لكون الحد الأوسط» إما محمولاً الأولى (۲) موضوعاً في الصغرى»، وهو في الشكل الطبيعي، وهو ينتج المطالب الأربعة الجزئي والكلي، والإيجابي، والسلبي وإما أن يكون «الأوسط» محمولاً فيهما، وهو الثاني، ولا ينتج إلا السلب. وإما أن يكون موضوعاً فيهما، ولا ينتج إلا الجزئيات والرابع ينتج الجزئيات والسلب الكلي، لكنه بعيد عن الطبع . ثم إذا أرادوا بيان إنتاج الثاني والثالث وغير ذلك من المطالب، احتاجوا إلى الاستدلال بـ «النقيض»، و «العكس»، و «عكس النقيض فإنه يلزم من صدق القضية كذب «نقيضها»، وصِدْقُ عكسها المستوى و عکس نقيضها». فإذا صَدَق قولنا : ليس أحد من الحجاج بكافر، صح قولنا : ليس أحد من الكفار حاجا». وإذا صح قولنا : كل حاج مسلم صح قولنا بعض المسلمين حاج، وقولنا : من ليس بمسلم فليس بحاج . (۱) في أصلنا : «و»، والتصحيح من «س».
Then they divided the categorical syllogism into the four figures because the middle term is either a predicate in the major and subject in the minor, which is the natural figure, and it produces the four requests: particular and universal, affirmative and negative; or it is that the middle is predicate in both, which is the second, and it produces only negation; or it is that it is subject in both, and it produces only particulars; and the fourth produces particulars and universal negation, but it is far from the natural disposition. Then when they wanted to demonstrate the production of the second and third and other requests, they needed reasoning by 'contradictory', 'conversion', and 'contrapositive' – for from the truth of a proposition follows the falsity of its contradictory, and the truth of its simple conversion and its contrapositive. So if our statement 'No pilgrim is an unbeliever' is true, then our statement 'No unbeliever is a pilgrim' is true. And if our statement 'Every pilgrim is a Muslim' is true, then our statement 'Some Muslims are pilgrims' is true, and our statement 'Whoever is not a Muslim is not a pilgrim.' (Footnote: In our original: 'and', and the correction from 's'.)
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage presents the classification of categorical syllogisms into four figures based on the position of the middle term. Ibn Taymiyya reports the logicians' scheme: In the first figure (natural), the middle term is predicate in the major premise and subject in the minor; this figure yields all four types of conclusions (universal/particular, affirmative/negative). The second figure has the middle term predicate in both premises, producing only negative conclusions. The third figure has the middle term subject in both, producing only particular conclusions. The fourth figure produces particulars and universal negation, but is considered unnatural (ba'id 'an al-tab'). To derive conclusions in the second, third, and fourth figures directly, logicians use three operations: naqid (contradictory inference), 'aks (simple conversion), and 'aks al-naqid (contrapositive). The rule: if a proposition is true, its contradictory is false; the simple conversion (subject and predicate swapped) is true under specific conditions; the contrapositive is also true. Examples are given using hajj (pilgrimage): 'No pilgrim is an unbeliever' converts to 'No unbeliever is a pilgrim' (simple conversion). 'Every pilgrim is a Muslim' converts to 'Some Muslims are pilgrims' (conversion per accidens) and contrapositively to 'Whoever is not a Muslim is not a pilgrim.' These operations allow the logician to transform propositions into equivalent forms to complete a syllogism. The footnote indicates a textual correction. In Islamic intellectual tradition, these methods are used to test arguments about religious obligations and beliefs.

Key Terms
الشكل الطبيعي
الحد الأوسط
الشكل الثاني

الشكل الرابع

Immediate Inferences: Naqid, 'Aks, and 'Aks al-Naqid
Introduction of naqid, 'aks, and 'aks al-naqid as immediate inference tools.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to perform the three immediate inferences—contradictory, simple conversion, and contrapositive—on a categorical proposition and verify their truth preservation using Islamic examples.

Core Idea
Immediate inferences transform a proposition into an equivalent form essential for adapting premises to fit the first figure.

The passage introduces three operations—naqid (contradictory), 'aks (simple conversion), and 'aks al-naqid (contrapositive)—that allow logicians to derive new true propositions from a given one. These operations are crucial when a syllogism is not in the first figure. Let's learn each operation using the pilgrim/unbeliever examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition's type (A, E, I, O)
Determine the quantity (universal/particular) and quality (affirmative/negative) of the given proposition. For example, 'No pilgrim is an unbeliever' is a universal negative (E).
The operations depend on the proposition type.
2
Apply naqid (contradictory)
The contradictory of a true proposition is false. To form the contradictory, change the quality and switch to the opposite quantity? Actually, the contradictory has opposite truth value but same subject and predicate; it is the proposition that directly opposes. For A: O is contradictory; for E: I is contradictory; for I: E; for O: A.
The passage states: 'from the truth of a proposition follows the falsity of its contradictory.'
3
Apply 'aks (simple conversion)
Swap subject and predicate. For E and I, the truth is preserved. For A, the conversion yields an I (conversion per accidens). For O, simple conversion is not valid. Example: 'No pilgrim is an unbeliever' (E) converts to 'No unbeliever is a pilgrim' (E).
The passage gives this exact example.
4
Apply 'aks al-naqid (contrapositive)
Form the contrapositive by negating both terms and swapping. For A and O, the truth is preserved. For E, contrapositive yields O? Actually, the contrapositive of 'Every pilgrim is a Muslim' (A) is 'Whoever is not a Muslim is not a pilgrim' (A).
The passage provides this example: 'Every pilgrim is a Muslim' yields 'Whoever is not a Muslim is not a pilgrim.'
5
Verify truth preservation
Ensure that the new proposition logically follows and is true if the original is true. Use the given examples to check: If 'No pilgrim is an unbeliever' is true, then 'No unbeliever is a pilgrim' must also be true.
This is the key property that makes these inferences useful in syllogistic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قسَّموا «الاقتراني» إلى الأشكال الأربعة لكون الحد الأوسط» إما محمولاً الأولى (۲) موضوعاً في الصغرى»، وهو في الشكل الطبيعي، وهو ينتج المطالب الأربعة الجزئي والكلي، والإيجابي، والسلبي وإما أن يكون «الأوسط» محمولاً فيهما، وهو الثاني، ولا ينتج إلا السلب. وإما أن يكون موضوعاً فيهما، ولا ينتج إلا الجزئيات والرابع ينتج الجزئيات والسلب الكلي، لكنه بعيد عن الطبع . ثم إذا أرادوا بيان إنتاج الثاني والثالث وغير ذلك من المطالب، احتاجوا إلى الاستدلال بـ «النقيض»، و «العكس»، و «عكس النقيض فإنه يلزم من صدق القضية كذب «نقيضها»، وصِدْقُ عكسها المستوى و عکس نقيضها». فإذا صَدَق قولنا : ليس أحد من الحجاج بكافر، صح قولنا : ليس أحد من الكفار حاجا». وإذا صح قولنا : كل حاج مسلم صح قولنا بعض المسلمين حاج، وقولنا : من ليس بمسلم فليس بحاج . (۱) في أصلنا : «و»، والتصحيح من «س».
Then they divided the categorical syllogism into the four figures because the middle term is either a predicate in the major and subject in the minor, which is the natural figure, and it produces the four requests: particular and universal, affirmative and negative; or it is that the middle is predicate in both, which is the second, and it produces only negation; or it is that it is subject in both, and it produces only particulars; and the fourth produces particulars and universal negation, but it is far from the natural disposition. Then when they wanted to demonstrate the production of the second and third and other requests, they needed reasoning by 'contradictory', 'conversion', and 'contrapositive' – for from the truth of a proposition follows the falsity of its contradictory, and the truth of its simple conversion and its contrapositive. So if our statement 'No pilgrim is an unbeliever' is true, then our statement 'No unbeliever is a pilgrim' is true. And if our statement 'Every pilgrim is a Muslim' is true, then our statement 'Some Muslims are pilgrims' is true, and our statement 'Whoever is not a Muslim is not a pilgrim.' (Footnote: In our original: 'and', and the correction from 's'.)
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage presents the classification of categorical syllogisms into four figures based on the position of the middle term. Ibn Taymiyya reports the logicians' scheme: In the first figure (natural), the middle term is predicate in the major premise and subject in the minor; this figure yields all four types of conclusions (universal/particular, affirmative/negative). The second figure has the middle term predicate in both premises, producing only negative conclusions. The third figure has the middle term subject in both, producing only particular conclusions. The fourth figure produces particulars and universal negation, but is considered unnatural (ba'id 'an al-tab'). To derive conclusions in the second, third, and fourth figures directly, logicians use three operations: naqid (contradictory inference), 'aks (simple conversion), and 'aks al-naqid (contrapositive). The rule: if a proposition is true, its contradictory is false; the simple conversion (subject and predicate swapped) is true under specific conditions; the contrapositive is also true. Examples are given using hajj (pilgrimage): 'No pilgrim is an unbeliever' converts to 'No unbeliever is a pilgrim' (simple conversion). 'Every pilgrim is a Muslim' converts to 'Some Muslims are pilgrims' (conversion per accidens) and contrapositively to 'Whoever is not a Muslim is not a pilgrim.' These operations allow the logician to transform propositions into equivalent forms to complete a syllogism. The footnote indicates a textual correction. In Islamic intellectual tradition, these methods are used to test arguments about religious obligations and beliefs.

Key Terms
النقيض
العكس
عكس النقيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) No unbeliever is a pilgrim
B) Some pilgrims are unbelievers
C) Every unbeliever is a pilgrim
D) Some unbelievers are not pilgrims

Key Takeaway
Naqid, 'aks, and 'aks al-naqid are truth-preserving transformations that allow logicians to adapt propositions to fit the first figure and derive conclusions from non-first-figure syllogisms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the proposition 'No believer is a kafir', write its simple conversion and its contrapositive. Check whether each is valid and true if the original is true.

Take the proposition 'Some scholars are not wealthy' (O). Perform naqid (contradictory) and explain why simple conversion is not valid for O.
