Critique of Disjunctive Syllogisms: Disguised Conditionals
Author critiques logicians' use of disjunctive statements with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a disjunctive statement is actually a conditional relation and explain why it fails as a genuine disjunction.

Core Idea
Genuine disjunctions require exclusive alternatives; many logical examples are disguised conditionals.

Logicians often present disjunctive syllogisms as if they offer two genuine alternatives. However, the author Ibn Taymiyya demonstrates that many of their examples, such as 'This is alive or not knowing' or 'This is purified or not praying,' are not true disjunctions. Instead, they express a conditional relation where one term is a condition for the other. In this lesson, we will dissect these examples to see how the supposed disjunction collapses into a conditional, and understand why this critique matters for reasoning in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive statement
Locate the 'or' (أو) in the passage. For example, 'This is alive, or not knowing' and 'This is purified, or not praying.' These are presented as disjunctions.
The disjunction appears to offer two alternatives, but examine their relationship.
2
Recognize the condition-conditioned relationship
Note that in each example, one term is a condition (shart) for the other. For instance, life is a condition for knowledge; purity is a condition for prayer. The conditioned (mashrut) cannot exist without the condition.
The condition must be present for the conditioned to exist.
3
See the collapse into a conditional
The disjunction 'conditioned exists OR condition does not exist' is actually a conditional: 'If the condition did not exist, then the conditioned does not exist.' This is a logical tautology, not a genuine alternative.
The statement does not exclude both being false; it only says the conditioned cannot exist without the condition.
4
Conclude it's not a genuine disjunction
A genuine disjunction (especially exclusive) requires that exactly one of the alternatives is true. Here, both alternatives can be false (if condition exists and conditioned does not), so it fails as a disjunction.
The author argues such statements are unproductive for syllogistic reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأمثال كثيرة، كقولنا : هذا حي ، أو ليس بعالم، أو قادر، أو سميع، أو بصير، أو متكلم. فإنه إن وجدت الحياة فهو أحد القسمين، وإن عُدمت عدمت هذه الصفات، وقد يكون حيّاً من لا يوصف بذلك. وكذلك إذا قيل: «هذا متطهر، أو ليس بمصلّ»، فإنه إن عُدمت الصلاة عُدمت الطهارة، وإن وُجدت الطهارة فهو القسم الآخر، فلا يخلو الأمر منهما. وكذلك كلُّ عَدَم شرط و وجودِ مشروطه. فإنه إذا رُدّد الأمر بين وجود المشروط وعدم الشرط كان ذلك مانعاً من الخلق. فإنه لا يخلو الأمر من وجود الشرط وعدمه، وإذا عُدم عُدم الشرط. فصار الأمر لا يخلو من وجود المشروط وعدم الشرط.
And examples are many, like our statement: 'This is alive, or not knowing, or powerful, or hearing, or seeing, or speaking.' For if life exists, it is one of the two divisions, and if it is absent, these attributes are absent, and there may be a living being not described with that. And similarly when it is said: 'This is purified, or not praying,' for if prayer is absent, purification is absent, and if purification exists, it is the other division, so the matter is not free from them. And likewise every absence of a condition and existence of its conditioned. For if the matter is disjuncted between existence of the conditioned and absence of the condition, that prevents creation. For the matter is not free from existence of the condition and its absence, and when it is absent, the condition is absent. So the matter is not free from existence of the conditioned and absence of the condition.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage critiques the logicians' use of disjunctive statements. The author points out that in their examples, the disjunction is not exclusive but rather a conditional expression. For instance, saying 'This is alive or not knowing' is not a true disjunction because life is a condition for knowledge; the second part 'not knowing' includes cases where life is absent. The author shows that the logical structure actually amounts to 'either the conditioned exists or the condition does not exist,' which is a tautology and does not produce new knowledge. He illustrates with examples from theology (God's attributes) and fiqh (purity and prayer). The key point is that these disjunctions are not productive of new information; they only express a necessary relation between condition and conditioned.

Key Terms
الشرط
المشروط

B) It is a conditional relation: if purification exists, then prayer is present; if prayer is absent, purification is absent.

Key Takeaway
Many supposed disjunctions in logical examples are actually conditionals in disguise, and recognizing this prevents empty reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Four Figures of Categorical Syllogism
Author classifies the four figures of categorical syllogism and their productivity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four figures of categorical syllogism and determine which types of conclusions each figure can produce.

Core Idea
The position of the middle term determines the figure and its logical output.

To use categorical syllogisms effectively in Islamic reasoning, you must know the four figures and their strengths. The first figure is the only one that can yield all four types of conclusions. The others have limitations. This lesson unpacks the classification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defining feature
Note that the figures are distinguished solely by the position of the middle term (al-hadd al-awsat) in the two premises.
2
Learn the first figure
First figure: middle term is subject in minor premise and predicate in major premise. It is called 'natural' and produces all four types (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative).
3
Learn the second figure
Second figure: middle term is predicate in both premises. It produces only negative conclusions (universal negative or particular negative).
4
Learn the third figure
Third figure: middle term is subject in both premises. It produces only particular conclusions (particular affirmative or particular negative).
5
Learn the fourth figure
Fourth figure: middle term is predicate in minor and subject in major. It produces particulars and universal negative, but is considered 'far from nature' (ba'id 'an al-tab').
Memorize the two positions per figure: (S=subject, P=predicate, M=middle).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قسموا «الاقتراني» إلى الأشكال الأربعة لكون الحد الأوسط إما محمولاً في الكبرى موضوعاً في الصغرى، وهو الشكل الطبيعي، وهو ينتج المطالب الأربعة الجزئي والكلي، والإيجابي، والسلبي. وإما أن يكون الأوسط محمولاً فيهما، وهو الثاني، ولا ينتج إلا السلب. وإما أن يكون موضوعاً فيهما، ولا ينتج إلا الجزئيات. والرابع ينتج الجزئيات والسلب الكلي، لكنه بعيد عن الطبع.
Then they divided the categorical (syllogism) into four figures, because the middle term is either a predicate in the major and subject in the minor, which is the natural figure, and it produces the four demands: particular and universal, affirmative and negative. Or the middle is predicate in both, which is the second, and it produces only the negative. Or it is subject in both, and it produces only particulars. And the fourth produces particulars and universal negative, but it is far from nature.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage classifies the four figures of categorical syllogism. The first figure (middle term subject in minor, predicate in major) is the only one that can yield all four types of conclusion (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative). The second figure (middle predicate in both) yields only negative conclusions. The third figure (middle subject in both) yields only particular conclusions. The fourth figure yields particulars and universal negative but is considered unnatural. This classification is standard in Aristotelian logic as transmitted in Islamic tradition. The student should memorize the positions of the middle term for each figure.

Key Terms
الاقتراني (al-iqtirani): categorical syllogism
الحد الأوسط (al-hadd al-awsat): middle term
الشكل (al-shakl): figure

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) First figure
B) Second figure
C) Third figure
D) Fourth figure

Key Takeaway
The position of the middle term determines the figure, and only the first figure can produce all four types of conclusions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the syllogism: 'The world is temporally originated. Everything temporally originated has a cause. Therefore the world has a cause.' Identify the figure and the position of the middle term. Which figure is this, and which types of conclusions can it produce?

The Conditional Syllogism as Implication: Ibn Taymiyya's Critique
Author shows that the conditional syllogism reduces to implication and criticizes narrowness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the core implication relation in any expression of the conditional syllogism and apply it to Islamic juristic examples.

Core Idea
The conditional syllogism is just one expression of a universal implication relation.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya refutes the logicians' claim that the conditional syllogism (al-sharṭī al-muttaṣil) is a distinct logical form. He demonstrates that its essence is the implication (talāzum) between the implicans (malzūm) and the consequent (lāzim), a relation that can be expressed in multiple ways. This lesson will guide you through his argument and help you recognize the same logical structure in various Islamic legal formulations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim being refined
The logicians treat the conditional syllogism as a unique form with special rules. Ibn Taymiyya states that what they call 'conditional conjunctive' has a specific content: reasoning from affirmation of implicans to affirmation of consequent, and from negation of consequent to negation of implicans.
This is modus ponens and modus tollens in essence.
2
Extract the core implication relation
The core is the relation of talāzum (implication): if the implicans (malzūm) holds, the consequent (lāzim) holds; if the consequent does not hold, the implicans does not hold. This is a single logical relation.
It does not depend on any particular wording.
3
Recognize the multiple expressions of the same meaning
Ibn Taymiyya lists several ways to express this relation: as a conditional ('if...then'), as a categorical ('every A is B'), as a condition ('A is a condition for B'), and as a negated universal ('no A without B'). All convey the same intellectual meaning.
The variation shows the logicians' narrow focus on one form is misguided.
4
Understand the critique of narrowness
Ibn Taymiyya digresses to criticize the Greek logicians for confining themselves to a single expression, causing their minds and tongues to be constrained. He contrasts this with the expansive expression of those who grasp the true meaning.
This critique ties into his larger argument that natural human reasoning is superior to artificial logical systems.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العبارات. فإن ما يسمونه الشرطي المتصل مضمونه الاستدلال بثبوت الملزوم على ثبوت اللازم، وبانتفاء اللازم على انتفاء الملزوم، سواء عُبّر عن هذا المعنى بصيغة الشرط أو لا يغير بصيغة الجزم واختلاف صيغ الدليل مع اتحاد معناه لا يغير حقيقته. والكلام إنما هو حقيقة المعنى في المعاني العقلية، لا في الألفاظ . فإذا قال القائل: إن كانت الصلاةُ صحيحة فالمصلّي متطهر»؛ و «إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود ؛ و إن كان الفاعل عالماً قادراً فهو حي»؛ ونحو ذلك، فهذا معنى قوله : صحة الصلاة تستلزم صحة الطهارة»، وقوله : يلزم من صحة الصلاة صحة ثبوت الطهارة»، وقوله : (لا يكون مصلياً إلا مع الطهارة ، وقوله : «الطهارة شرط في صحة الصلاة، وإذا عُدم الشرط عُدم المشروط»، وقوله : «كل مصل متطهر، فمن ليس بمتطهر فليس بمصل». وأمثال ذلك من أنواع التأليف للألفاظ والمعاني التي يتضمن هذا الاستدلال من غير حَصْر الناس في عبارة واحدة . استطراد. الركة والعِيُّ وإذا اتسعت العقولُ وتصوراتها اتسعت عباراتها، وإذا ضاقت العقول والتصورات بقي صاحبها كأنه محبوس العقل واللسان، كما يصيب أهل المنطق اليوناني - تجدهم من أضيق الناس علماً وبياناً، وأعجزهم تصوراً وتعبيراً. ولهذا من كان منهم ذكياً إذا تصرّف في العلوم، وسَلَك مسلك أهل المنطق، طَوَّلَ
Expressions. For what they call the conditional conjunctive — its content is reasoning by the affirmation of the implicans to the affirmation of the necessary consequent, and by the negation of the consequent to the negation of the implicans, whether this meaning is expressed in the conditional form or the affirmative form, and the variation in the forms of evidence with the unity of its meaning does not change its reality. Speech is only about the reality of meaning in intellectual meanings, not in words. So when one says: 'If the prayer is valid, then the worshiper is pure'; and 'If the sun has risen, then daytime exists'; and 'If the agent is knowing and powerful, he is living'; and so on, that is the meaning of his saying: 'The validity of prayer necessitates the validity of purity'; and his saying: 'It follows from the validity of prayer the affirmation of purity'; and his saying: 'One is not a praying person except with purity'; and his saying: 'Purity is a condition for the validity of prayer, and if the condition is absent, the conditioned is absent'; and his saying: 'Every praying person is pure, so whoever is not pure is not a praying person.' And the like of these are types of compositions of words and meanings that contain this reasoning without restricting people to a single expression. Digression: Constriction and incapacity. And if minds and their conceptions expand, their expressions expand. And if minds and conceptions narrow, their possessor remains as if imprisoned in mind and tongue, as happens to the Greek logicians — you find them among the most confined of people in knowledge and expression, and the most incapable in conception and expression. Therefore, whoever among them is intelligent, if he acts in the sciences and follows the path of the logicians, he prolongs...
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the logicians' understanding of the conditional syllogism (al-sharṭī al-muttaṣil). Ibn Taymiyya begins by stating that what the logicians call the 'conditional conjunctive' is essentially reasoning by affirming the implicans (malzūm) to affirm the consequent (lāzim), or negating the consequent to negate the implicans. He emphasizes that this logical form is not unique; the same meaning can be expressed in many ways: as a conditional ('if...then'), as a categorical statement ('every prayer is valid implies purity'), or as a statement of condition ('purity is a condition for prayer'). He gives several examples from Islamic jurisprudence (prayer and purity) to illustrate that the underlying intellectual meaning—the implication relation—is what matters, not the specific wording. The author then launches a digression (istitrād) criticizing the Greek logicians for their narrow-mindedness and incapacity in expression. He argues that they constrain themselves to rigid forms, whereas the reality of reasoning is about meanings, not words. This reinforces his broader argument that the logicians' system is not superior to the natural human reasoning used by Muslim scholars.

Key Terms
الشرطي المتصل
الملزوم
اللازم
المعنى العقلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Reasoning by affirming the antecedent and negating the consequent.
B) Reasoning by affirming the implicans to affirm the consequent and negating the consequent to negate the implicans.
C) Reasoning by analogy (tamthīl).
D) Reasoning by induction (istiqrāʾ).

Key Takeaway
The essence of the conditional syllogism is the implication relation, not its linguistic form.

5 min
The First Figure of Categorical Syllogisms is Innate
Claims that the first figure suffices and is innate, not needing instruction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the claim that the first figure of categorical syllogisms is innate and evaluate its implications for the necessity of formal logic instruction.

Core Idea
The first figure is innate, so formal logic instruction is not needed for sound reasoning.

This passage returns to the main topic and delivers a powerful critique: even the logicians themselves concede that the first figure of categorical syllogisms is sufficient for all valid reasoning and that its formulation is innate (fitri). This means humans naturally reason in this form without needing to learn it from logicians. The author uses this concession to argue that the entire formal apparatus of logic is unnecessary for correct reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim about the first figure
Locate the statement that the first figure suffices for all forms of qiyas. What does 'yughni' (suffices) imply about the other figures?
The author is quoting the logicians' own admission.
2
Recognize the logicians' concession
Note that the phrase 'wa hum mu'tarifūn' indicates the logicians themselves acknowledge this. Why is this a powerful rhetorical move?
It undermines the need for their own elaborate system.
3
Understand 'fitri' (innate)
The passage says 'taswīruh fitrī' - its formulation is innate. What does this mean about human reasoning? Does it require external instruction?
Innate means natural, not acquired through formal teaching.
4
Connect the critique to the overall argument
The author concludes that reasoning does not need to be cast into the logicians' technical mold. How does this critique weaken the claim that logic is an indispensable tool?
If the basic form is innate, then elaborate rules are superfluous.

Ibn Taymiyya on the Innateness of the First Figure
Ibn Taymiyya asserts that the first figure of categorical syllogism is innate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that the first syllogistic figure is innate and evaluate how it undermines the claim that formal logic is necessary for sound reasoning.

Core Idea
The first figure of syllogism is innate; logicians teach what humans already know.

Ibn Taymiyya cites the logicians' own admission that the first figure suffices for all syllogisms. He then declares it innate (fitri), meaning it is natural to human reason and does not require formal instruction. This is a key move in his critique of the logicians' claim that their discipline is epistemologically foundational. In this lesson, we unpack his concise but powerful argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core claim
In the Arabic, Ibn Taymiyya says: 'وَتَصْوِيرُهُ فِطْرِيٌّ لَا يَحْتَاجُ أَحَدٌ إِلَى تَعَلُّمِهِ مِنْهُمْ' — its formulation is innate, no one needs to learn it from them. Locate this sentence and notice how it directly follows the logicians' admission.
2
Understand 'fitri' (innate)
Fitri means natural, intuitive, built into human reasoning. Ibn Taymiyya means that every sound human mind naturally reasons in the pattern of the first figure (e.g., a is b, b is c, so a is c). This does not require studying Aristotle or al-Qazwini.
Contrast this with the logicians' view that the first figure is the only perfect figure, but still needs to be learned as part of the discipline.
3
Connect to the logicians' admission
The first part of the passage says: 'وَهُمْ مُعْتَرِفُونَ بِأَنَّ الشَّكْلَ الْأَوَّلَ مِنَ الْحَمْلِيَّاتِ يُغْنِي عَنْ جَمِيعِ صُوَرِ الْقِيَاسِ' — they acknowledge that the first figure of categoricals suffices for all forms of syllogism. Ibn Taymiyya uses this concession to argue that if the first figure is both sufficient and innate, then the entire enterprise of formal logic is redundant.
4
Assess the final point about inference
He adds: 'مَعَ أَنَّ الِاسْتِدْلَالَ لَا يَحْتَاجُ إِلَى تَصَوُّرِهِ عَلَى الْوَجْهِ الَّذِي يَزْعُمُونَهُ' — inference does not need to be formulated in the way they claim. This means that even the conceptualization of the first figure in technical terms is unnecessary; natural reasoning suffices.
This is a radical challenge to the logicians' pedagogical and epistemological program.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عود إلى أصل الموضوع : و هم معترفون بأن الشكل الأول من الحمليات يُغني عن جميع صور «القياس». وتصويره فطري لا يحتاج أحد إلى تعلمه منهم، مع أن الاستدلال لا يحتاج إلى تصوّره على الوجه الذي يزعمونه .
Return to the original subject: And they acknowledge that the first figure of categoricals suffices for all forms of 'syllogism'. And its formulation is innate (fitri), not requiring anyone to learn it from them, while inference does not need to be formulated in the way they claim.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This short passage is a conclusion from earlier discussion. Ibn Taymiyya returns to the main topic and points out that the logicians themselves admit that the first figure of categorical syllogism (where the middle term is subject in one premise and predicate in the other) is sufficient for all types of syllogism. He then adds that this figure is innate (fitri) – meaning it is a natural, intuitive reasoning pattern – and therefore does not require any formal training from logicians. Moreover, inference does not need to be explicitly cast into their technical form. This supports his overall critique that formal logic is unnecessary for correct reasoning.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya argues that the first syllogistic figure is natural and does not require formal logic, undermining the claim that logic is necessary for sound inference.

Ibn Taymiyya's Refutation of the Two-Premise Requirement
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the rigid two-premise requirement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the number of premises in an inference is not fixed and provide examples from Islamic fiqh that illustrate varying premise needs.

Core Idea
The number of premises needed depends on the knower, not the logic.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges the logicians' claim that every inference must have exactly two premises. He argues that the number varies based on prior knowledge, using examples from fiqh such as intoxicants and gambling. This lesson explores his critique and its implications for practical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the logicians' claim
The logicians assert that reasoning needs exactly two premises: if one premise appears alone, they say the other is implicit (qiyas al-damir); if more than two, they call it compound syllogisms. This is their attempt to enforce a two-premise rule.
Ibn Taymiyya rejects this as artificial.
2
Understand Ibn Taymiyya's counter
He says the need for premises varies: some know by necessity, some need one, two, three, or more premises. The number is not fixed.
He emphasizes the variability of human knowledge.
3
Analyze the intoxicant example
If someone knows the universal rule 'every intoxicant is prohibited' but does not know whether a specific drink is intoxicant, they need only one premise—that the drink is intoxicant—to conclude it is prohibited.
This shows one-premise inference is sufficient when the major premise is known.
4
Extend to other fiqh disputes
Ibn Taymiyya gives examples: whether chess is gambling, whether nabidh is wine, whether certain oaths fall under Quranic verses, and whether 'he in whose hand is the marriage contract' refers to husband or guardian. In each, the number of premises needed depends on what the person already knows.
These examples demonstrate the practical variability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: إبطال قولهم : إن الاستدلال لا بد فيه من مقدمتين بلا زيادة ولا نقصان، فإن كان «الدليل» مقدمة واحدة قالوا: «الأخرى محذوفة»، وسَمَّوه هو «قياس الضمير». وإن كان مقدمات قالوا: هي أقيسة مركبة، ليس هو قياساً واحداً». فهذا قول باطل طرداً وعكساً. وذلك أن احتياج المستدل إلى المقدّمات مما يختلف فيه حالُ الناس . فمن الناس من لا يحتاج إلا إلى مقدمة واحدة لعلمه بما سوى ذلك. كما أن منهم من لا يحتاج في علمه بذلك إلى استدلال، بل قد يعلمه بالضرورة. ومنهم من يحتاج إلى مقدمتين، ومنهم من يحتاج إلى ثلاث، ومنهم من يحتاج إلى أربع وأكثر. فمن أراد أن يعرف أن هذا المسكر المعيَّنَ محرّم، فإن كان يعرف أن «كل مسكر حرام» ولكن لا يعرف هل هذا المعين مسكر أم لا؟، لم يحتج إلا إلى مقدمة واحدة، وهو أن يعلم أن هذا مسكر . فإذا قيل له : «هذا حرام» ، فقال «ما الدليل عليه؟»، فقال المستدل : الدليل على ذلك أنه مسكر ، فقال : لا نسلّم أنه مسكر»؛ فمتى أقام الدليل على أنه مسكر تم المطلوب . وكذلك لو تنازع اثنان في بعض أنواع الأشربة هل هو مسكر أم لا؟ كما يسأل الناسُ كثيراً عن بعض الأشربة، فلا يكون السائل ممن يعلم أنها تسكر أو لا تسكر، ولكن قد علم أن كل مسكر حرام. فإذا ثبت عنده بخبرٍ مَنْ يصدّقه، أو بغير ذلك من الأدلة، أنه مسكر، علم تحريمه . وكذلك سائر ما يقع الشك في اندراجه تحت قضية كلية من الأنواع والأعيان، مع العلم بحكم تلك القضية، كتنازع الناس في النَّرْد والشَّطْرَنْج «هل هما من المَيْسِر أم لا؟»؛ وتنازُعِهم في النبيذ المتنازع فيه هل هو من الخمر أم لا؟»؛ وتنازعهم في الحلف بالنذر، والطلاق، والعتاق «هل هو داخل في قوله تعالى : قد فَرَضَ اللَّهُ لَكُمْ تَحِلَّةَ أَيْمَنِكُمْ ﴾ - أم لا؟»؛ وتنازعهم في قوله: ﴿ أَوْ يَعْفُوا الَّذِى بِيَدِهِ، عُقْدَةُ النِّكَاح ﴾ - هل هو الزوج أو الولي المستقل؟»؛ وأمثال ذلك . وقد يحتاج الاستدلال إلى مقدمتين كمن لم يعلم أن النبيذ المسكر المتنازع فيه محرم ولم يعلم أن هذا المعيَّن مسكر . فهو لا يعلم أنه محرم حتى يعلم أنه مسكر .
Section: Refutation of their statement that inference necessarily requires two premises, no more and no less. For if the evidence was one premise, they say: 'the other is omitted', and they call it 'syllogism of the conscience'. And if it was multiple premises, they say: 'these are compound syllogisms, not a single syllogism'. So this statement is false in both its directions. That is because the need of the inferer for premises differs according to the condition of people. Among people are those who need only one premise due to their knowledge of what is beyond that. Just as among them are those who do not need inference for that knowledge, but rather know it necessarily. And among them are those who need two premises, and among them are those who need three, and among them are those who need four and more. So whoever wants to know that this specific intoxicant is prohibited, if he knows that 'every intoxicant is prohibited' but does not know whether this specific one is intoxicant or not, he needs only one premise, which is to know that this is intoxicant. So if it is said to him: 'this is prohibited', and he says: 'what is the evidence?', the inferer says: 'the evidence for that is that it is intoxicant', and he says: 'we do not accept that it is intoxicant'; when he provides evidence that it is intoxicant, the desired result is complete. Similarly, if two people dispute about some types of beverages whether it is intoxicant or not, as people often ask about some beverages, so the questioner is not among those who know that they intoxicate or not, but he knows that every intoxicant is prohibited. So if it is established to him by the report of one he trusts, or by other evidence, that it is intoxicant, he knows its prohibition. Likewise all that is doubted to fall under a universal proposition from types and individuals, while knowing the ruling of that proposition, like people's dispute about backgammon and chess 'are they from gambling or not?'; and their dispute about the disputed nabidh 'is it from wine or not?'; and their dispute about swearing by vows, divorce, and manumission 'is it included in His statement: 'Allah has already ordained for you the dissolution of your oaths' or not?'; and their dispute about His statement: 'or he in whose hand is the marriage contract' - is it the husband or the independent guardian?; and the like. And inference may need two premises, like one who does not know that the intoxicating nabidh under dispute is prohibited and does not know that this specific one is intoxicant. So he does not know that it is prohibited until he knows that it is intoxicant.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the logicians' claim that every inference must have exactly two premises. Ibn Taymiyya first states their position: if only one premise is used, they call the missing premise implicit and term it 'syllogism of the conscience'; if more than two premises are used, they claim it is a compound of multiple syllogisms. He declares this false in both directions. Then he provides the real reason: the number of premises needed varies from person to person depending on their prior knowledge. He lists possibilities: some need no inference (necessary knowledge), some need one premise, some two, three, four, or more. He illustrates with an example from fiqh: someone who knows the universal rule 'every intoxicant is prohibited' but does not know whether a specific drink is intoxicant needs only one premise—proving that it is intoxicant. He then gives examples of disputes about the applicability of universal rules, such as whether backgammon and chess are gambling, whether nabidh is wine, whether certain oaths fall under Quranic verses, and whether the phrase 'he in whose hand is the marriage contract' refers to the husband or the guardian. Finally, he gives an example requiring two premises: one who knows neither the universal rule nor the specific fact must learn both. Throughout, Ibn Taymiyya uses Islamic legal examples to show that the logicians' rigid two-premise requirement does not match actual reasoning.

Key Terms
مقدمة
قياس الضمير
دليل
مسكر

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because the logicians' rule of two premises does not apply to intoxicants.
D) Because one premise is sufficient for all legal inferences.

Key Takeaway
The number of premises required for inference is not fixed at two; it depends on the prior knowledge of the reasoner.

Clarifying 'Qawl' in the Logicians' Definition of Qiyas
Author explains the logicians' definition of qiyas as 'qawl' meaning complete propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between 'qawl' as a single word and 'qawl' as a complete proposition in the definition of qiyas, and correctly interpret the definition using Islamic examples.

Core Idea
In logic, 'qawl' means a proposition, not a word – confusing them undermines the definition of syllogism.

The logicians define qiyas (syllogism) as 'a qawl composed of aqwal, which if accepted necessarily entails another qawl'. But what exactly does 'qawl' mean here? Ibn Taymiyya clarifies that they intend complete propositions (qadiyya), not individual words like the terms of a syllogism. This distinction is crucial for understanding how a syllogism works and for evaluating its definition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Find the definition of qiyas in the text: 'qiyas is a qawl composed of aqwal'. The word 'qawl' is key – we need to determine its precise meaning.
2
Distinguish two possible meanings of 'qawl'
Consider two possibilities: (1) qawl as a single word (like 'qiyas' itself), or (2) qawl as a complete proposition (declarative sentence). The logicians explicitly reject (1) and confirm (2).
In other contexts, qawl can mean any speech, but here it is technical.
3
Apply to an example from the passage
Look at the example: 'The disputed nabidh is an intoxicant, and every intoxicant is forbidden.' The terms 'nabidh', 'intoxicant', 'forbidden' are single words, but the premises 'Nabidh is intoxicant' and 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' are complete propositions – these are the 'aqwal' that compose the qiyas.
The conclusion is also a proposition (e.g., 'Nabidh is forbidden').
4
Recognize the consequence for the definition
Therefore, the logicians call the whole syllogism a 'qawl' because it is composed of propositions (aqwal), each of which is a qawl. The terms are not the syllogism; the propositions are.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثالث - حدهم القياس، ونقضه للمصنف
والمقصود هنا أنهم أرادوا بـ «القول» في قولهم: القياس قول مؤلف من أقوال، المراد بـ «القول» القضية التي هي جملة تامة خبرية، لم يريدوا بذلك المفرد الذي هو «القياس» مشتمل على ثلاثة حدود أصغر وأوسط وأكبر كما إذا قيل: «النبيذ المتنازع فيه مسكر وكل مسكر حرام، فـ «النبيذ» و «المسكر» و «الحرام» كل منها مفرد، وهي الحدود في «القياس». فليس مرادهم بـ «القول» هذا. بل مرادهم أن «كلَّ قضية قول» كما فسروا مرادهم بذلك. ولهذا قالوا: قول مؤلف من أقوال إذا سُلّمت لزم عنها لذاتها قول آخر، واللازم إنما هو النتيجة، وهي قضية، وخبر، وجملة تامة؛ ليست مفرداً. ولذلك قالوا القياس قول، فسموا مجموع القضيتين قولاً.
The third station: Their definition of qiyas, and its refutation by the author. And the intended meaning here is that they intended by 'qawl' in their statement: 'qiyas is a qawl composed of aqwal', the intended meaning of 'qawl' is the proposition (qadiyya) which is a complete declarative sentence. They did not intend by that the single word which is 'qiyas' containing three terms: minor, middle, and major, as when it is said: 'The disputed nabidh is an intoxicant, and every intoxicant is forbidden'; so 'nabidh', 'intoxicant', and 'forbidden' are each single words, and they are the terms in the qiyas. So their intended meaning by 'qawl' is not this. Rather, their intended meaning is that every proposition is a 'qawl', as they explained their intention by that. And for this they said: 'A qawl composed of aqwal which, if accepted, necessarily entails by itself another qawl', and the entailed is the conclusion, which is a proposition, a predicate, and a complete sentence; not a single word. Therefore they called qiyas a 'qawl', so they called the sum of the two propositions a 'qawl'.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage clarifies the logicians' definition of qiyas (syllogism). The author (Ibn Taymiyya) explains that when they define qiyas as 'a qawl composed of aqwal', they do not mean a single word like 'qiyas' itself. Instead, by 'qawl' they refer to a complete proposition (qadiyya), which is a declarative sentence. The example given is: 'The disputed nabidh is an intoxicant, and every intoxicant is forbidden.' Here, 'nabidh', 'intoxicant', and 'forbidden' are single words, but the qiyas is composed of the two propositions (the two premises). The conclusion is also a proposition. Thus, the logicians call the combination of the two premises a 'qawl' because each premise is a 'qawl' (proposition). The key point is that 'qawl' in this definition means a proposition, not a word or term.

Key Terms
القول (al-qawl): In this definition, al-qawl means a complete proposition (qadiyya) or declarative sentence, not a single word.
القياس (al-qiyas): Defined as a qawl composed of aqwal (propositions) which, if accepted, necessarily entails another qawl (conclusion).
الحدود (al-hudud): The terms (singular: hadd) of a syllogism: minor (asghar), middle (awsat), and major (akbar).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) A single word
B) A term (hadd) in a syllogism
C) A complete proposition (qadiyya)
D) The conclusion of a syllogism

Key Takeaway
In the logicians' definition of qiyas, 'qawl' refers to a complete proposition; the syllogism is composed of propositions that together imply a conclusion.

How Many Premises Does a Syllogism Need? Ibn Taymiyya's Objection
Author critiques logicians' two-premise rule

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the logicians' justification for exactly two premises in a simple syllogism and identify Ibn Taymiyya's linguistic objection based on the meaning of 'plural'.

Core Idea
The logicians insist on two premises; Ibn Taymiyya argues the word 'aqwal' allows three or more.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya challenges the logicians' claim that a valid syllogism must consist of exactly two premises. He starts by examining the language of their definition: they say a syllogism is composed of 'aqwal' (plural of qawl, meaning propositions). The plural 'aqwal' could mean two or more, but the logicians restrict it to exactly two. Ibn Taymiyya uses this linguistic ambiguity to question their commitment, and then presents their justification based on the structure of the conclusion. This lesson unpacks that exchange.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' position on premises
Find where the author states that the logicians commit to exactly two premises for a simple syllogism, and that they say the minimum is two and it cannot exceed two.
Look for phrases like 'لا ينقص عن مقدمتين ولا يزيد عليهما'
2
Analyze Ibn Taymiyya's linguistic objection
He points out that the word 'plural' (jam') in their definition could mean 'two and above' or 'three and above'. He argues that if they meant the genus of number, it includes two and above, so a syllogism could have three or more premises. Note the Quranic example of 'ikhwa' (brothers) meaning two or more.
The Quranic verse 4:11 uses 'ikhwa' to mean two or more brothers.
3
Examine the logicians' justification
The logicians justify two premises by saying the conclusion has only two parts (subject and predicate). For a categorical syllogism, each part corresponds to a term in a premise. For a conditional syllogism, a conditional premise and an exceptive premise suffice. Understand this reasoning.
They argue that the matlub (conclusion) is not more than two juz' (parts).
4
Evaluate the force of Ibn Taymiyya's objection
Consider whether his linguistic point is a strong critique or whether the logicians can accommodate it by clarifying that they mean the minimum is two. Reflect on the tension between the definitional language and the actual practice.
Think about whether a syllogism in Islamic legal reasoning ever uses more than two premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا كانوا قد جعلوا القياس مؤلفاً من أقوال، وهي القضايا، لم يجب أن يراد الجمع يتناول بذلك قولان فقط. لأن لفظ الجمع إما أن يكون متناولاً لـ «اثنين فصاعداً»، كقوله: ﴿فإن كَانَ لَهُ إِخْوَةٌ فَلِأُمِّهِ السُّدُسُ﴾ [سورة النساء: ١١]، وإما أن يراد به «الثلاثة فصاعداً»، وهو الأصل عند الجمهور. ولكن قد يراد به جنس العدد فيتناول الاثنين فصاعداً، ولا يكون الجمع مختصاً بـ «اثنين». فإذا قالوا: هو مؤلف من أقوال، إن أرادوا جنس العدد كان المعنى من اثنين فصاعداً. فيجوز أن يكون مؤلفاً من ثلاث مقدمات، وأربع مقدمات؛ فلا يختص بالاثنين. وإن أرادوا الجمع الحقيقي لم يكن مؤلفاً إلا من «ثلاثة فصاعداً»، وهم قطعاً ما أرادوا هذا. لم يبق إلا الأول.
فإذا قيل: هم يلتزمون ذلك، ويقولون: نحن نقول: «أقل ما يكون القياس من مقدمتين، وقد يكون من مقدمات». فيقال: أولاً هذا خلافُ ما في كتبكم، فإنكم لا تلتزمون إلا مقدمتين فقط. وقد صرحوا أن القياس الموصل إلى المطلوب، سواء كان اقترانياً أو استثنائياً، لا ينقص عن مقدمتين ولا يزيد عليهما.
وعللوا ذلك بأن المطلوب المتحد لا يزيد على جزئين - مبتدأ وخبر. فإن كان القياس اقترانياً فكل واحد من جُزئي المطلوب لا بد وأن يناسب مقدمة منه، أي يكون فيها إما مبتدأ وإما خبراً، ولا يكون هو نفس المقدمة. قالوا: وليس المطلوب أكثر من جزئين، فلا يفتقر إلى أكثر من مقدمتين. وإن كان القياس استثنائياً فلا بد فيه من مقدمة - متصلة أو منفصلة - تكون مناسبة لكل مطلوب أو نقيضه، ولا بد من مقدمة استثنائية، فلا حاجة إلى ثالثة.
And since they made qiyas composed of aqwal, which are propositions, it is not necessary that the plural be taken to include only two statements. Because the word 'plural' is either applicable to 'two and above', as in His saying: 'But if he has brothers, then for his mother the sixth' [Surah An-Nisa 4:11], or it may be intended to mean 'three and above', which is the default according to the majority. But it may also be used to denote the genus of number, thus including two and above, and the plural is not specific to 'two'. So if they say: it is composed of aqwal, if they mean the genus of number, the meaning is from two and above. Then it is permissible for it to be composed of three premises, four premises, so it is not specific to two. And if they meant the real plural, it would not be composed except from three and above, and they definitely did not intend this. Nothing remains except the first. If it is said: they commit to that, and they say: 'We say: the minimum of qiyas is two premises, and it may be from premises.' Then it is said: first, this is contrary to what is in your books, for you do not commit to except only two premises. And they have stated that the qiyas that leads to the sought conclusion, whether it is iqtirani or istithna'i, does not fall short of two premises nor exceed them. And they justified that by saying that the sought conclusion, being single, does not exceed two parts - subject and predicate. For if the qiyas is iqtirani, each of the two parts of the sought conclusion must correspond to a premise from it, i.e., be in it either as subject or predicate, and it is not itself the premise. They said: and the sought conclusion is not more than two parts, so it does not need more than two premises. And if the qiyas is istithna'i, it must have a premise - conditional or disjunctive - that corresponds to each sought conclusion or its contradictory, and it must have an exceptive premise, so no third is needed.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage discusses the number of premises in a syllogism according to the logicians. The author (Ibn Taymiyya) first notes that the word 'plural' (jam') in their definition could mean two or more, as in the Quranic usage where 'ikhwa' (brothers) means two or more. They argue that if the logicians intend the plural in the sense of two or more, then a syllogism could have more than two premises. However, the author points out that the logicians actually commit to exactly two premises in their books, stating that a syllogism, whether categorical (iqtirani) or conditional (istithna'i), does not need more than two premises. Their justification is that the conclusion (al-matlub) has only two parts (subject and predicate), so each part is covered by one premise. For categorical syllogisms, each of the two parts of the conclusion corresponds to a term in one of the premises. For conditional syllogisms, a conditional premise plus an exceptive premise suffice, so no third premise is needed. This section shows the logicians' attempt to limit a simple syllogism to two premises, despite the possibility of more.

The logicians' claim of exactly two premises rests on the structure of the conclusion, but Ibn Taymiyya's linguistic critique shows that their own definitional language does not necessitate that limit.

Compound Syllogism and Ibn Taymiyya's Objection on the Unity of Judgment
Ibn Taymiyya critiques the logicians' compound syllogism concept.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's objection to the compound syllogism and explain why he argues that the conclusion is a single judgment in the mind, not two verbal parts.

Core Idea
For Ibn Taymiyya, a conclusion is one mental judgment, not two verbal parts—so the number of premises is not fixed at two.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya first presents the logicians' concept of the compound syllogism (al-qiyas al-murakkab), including its types and purposes. He then raises a fundamental objection: the logical conclusion in the mind is a single judgment—the affirmation or negation of a relational nexus (al-nisba al-hukmiyya)—not two separate parts. This challenges the logicians' rationale for a fixed two-premise structure and opens the door to a more flexible understanding of deduction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' definition of compound syllogism
Read the opening lines: they say additional statements beyond the two premises can be included, either unrelated or related. The related ones are for embellishment or clarifying premises. This is what they call 'compound syllogism'.
2
Distinguish between mawsul and mafsul
In the examples: mawsul (connected) does not state intermediate conclusions; mafsul (disconnected) explicitly states them. For instance, 'Every human is an animal, every animal is a body, every body is a substance, so every human is a substance' is mawsul. The mafsul version breaks it into steps, stating intermediate conclusions.
The OCR note suggests a correction in the Arabic example: the last line may be misread.
3
Locate Ibn Taymiyya's objection
After 'فيقال لهم', Ibn Taymiyya objects: the sought conclusion (al-matlub) verbally has two parts (subject and predicate), but in the mind it is one thing—the affirmation or denial of a judgmental relation (al-nisba al-hukmiyya).
4
Evaluate the objection's force
Ibn Taymiyya argues that since the mental conclusion is a single entity, the logicians' insistence that a syllogism must consist of exactly two premises (corresponding to the two verbal parts) is unwarranted. The mental unity of judgment allows for multiple premises contributing to one conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا: لكن ربما أدرج في القياس قول زائد على مقدمتي القياس، إما غير متعلق بالقياس، أو متعلق به. والمتعلق بالقياس إما لترويج الكلام وتحسينه، أو لبيان المقدمتين أو إحداهما، ويسمون هذا القياس المركب.
قالوا: وحاصله يرجع إلى أقيسة متعددة سيقت لبيان مطلوب واحد، إلا أن القياس المبين للمطلوب بالذات منها ليس إلا واحداً والباقي لبيان مقدمات القياس. قالوا: وربما حذفوا بعض مقدمات القياس، إما تعويلاً على فهم الذهن لها، أو لترويج المغلطة حتى لا يُطَّلع على كذبها عند التصريح بها.
قالوا: ثم إن كانت الأقيسة لبيان المقدمات قد صُرّح فيها بنتائجها فيسمى القياس «مفصولاً»، وإلا فـ «موصولاً».
ومثلوا الموصول بقول القائل: كل إنسان حيوان، وكل حيوان جسم، وكل جسم جوهر، فكل إنسان جوهر.
والمفصول بقولهم: كل إنسان حيوان وكل حيوان جسم، فكل إنسان جسم. ثم يقول: كل حيوان جسم، وكل جسم جوهر، فكل إنسان حيوان. فيلزم منهما أن كل إنسان جوهر.
فيقال لهم: أما المطلوب الذي لا يزيد على جزئين فذاك في النطق به. والمطلوب أو انتفاؤها في العقل إنما هو شيء واحد، لا اثنان. وهو ثبوت النسبة الحكمية أو انتفاؤها.
They said: But sometimes an additional statement is included in the qiyas beyond the two premises of the qiyas, either unrelated to the qiyas or related to it. And that which is related to the qiyas is either for embellishing speech and improving it, or for clarifying the two premises or one of them, and they call this the compound qiyas (al-qiyas al-murakkab). They said: Its essence reduces to multiple syllogisms brought to prove one sought conclusion, except that the syllogism that directly proves the sought conclusion is only one, and the rest are for clarifying the premises of the syllogism. They said: And sometimes they omit some premises of the syllogism, either relying on the mind's understanding of them, or for promoting a fallacy so that its falsehood is not detected when stated explicitly. They said: Then if the syllogisms for clarifying the premises have their results explicitly stated, the syllogism is called 'disconnected' (mafsul), otherwise 'connected' (mawsul). They gave as an example of mawsul the statement of one: Every human is an animal, every animal is a body, every body is a substance, so every human is a substance. And of mafsul their statement: Every human is an animal and every animal is a body, so every human is a body. Then he says: Every animal is a body, every body is a substance, so every human is an animal. So from these two it follows that every human is a substance. Then it is said to them: As for the sought conclusion not exceeding two parts, that is in verbal expression. The sought conclusion, or its negation, in the mind is only one thing, not two. It is the affirmation or negation of the judgmental relation.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage explains the logicians' concept of the compound syllogism (al-qiyas al-murakkab). They recognize that sometimes additional statements are included in a syllogism for embellishment, clarification, or even for concealing falsehood. The compound syllogism reduces to multiple syllogisms that serve to clarify the premises of the main syllogism. Two types are distinguished: 'connected' (mawsul) where the intermediate results are not explicitly stated, and 'disconnected' (mafsul) where they are. Examples are given: a connected syllogism is like 'Every human is an animal, every animal is a body, every body is a substance, so every human is a substance.' A disconnected syllogism is like first deriving 'Every human is a body' from the first two premises, then using that with another premise to derive the final conclusion. After presenting this, Ibn Taymiyya objects: the logicians claim that the conclusion consists of two parts (subject and predicate) verbally, but in the mind the conclusion is a single judgment—either the affirmation or negation of a relation (nisba hukmiyya). Thus, the existence of two verbal parts does not justify limiting the premises to two, as the mind grasps a single mental content. This critique challenges the logicians' justification of exactly two premises.

Key Takeaway

The Syllogism

Compound syllogism: definition and types (mawsul vs mafsun)
Authors define compound syllogism, distinguish mawsul and mafsun with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish a compound syllogism from a simple one, and identify whether it is mawsul or mafsun by checking for explicit sub-conclusions.

Core Idea
A compound syllogism is a chain of syllogisms; only one proves the main conclusion directly.

This passage from the logicians explains that a syllogism may include extra statements beyond two premises, forming a compound syllogism. It reduces to multiple syllogisms aimed at a single desired conclusion. Understanding how to unpack such chains is essential for evaluating complex arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence, where multi-step proofs are common.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main conclusion (natija)
Locate the final statement that the entire argument aims to prove. In the examples, it is 'Every human is a substance' (كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ جَوْهَرٌ).
2
Count the premises and identify extra statements
A simple syllogism has exactly two premises. If there are more than two statements that are not mere embellishments, the argument is compound. In the mawsul example, there are three premises: 'Every human is an animal', 'Every animal is a body', 'Every body is a substance'.
3
Determine the main syllogism
Among the multiple syllogisms, one directly yields the main conclusion. The rest are sub-syllogisms that prove one of its premises. Here, the main syllogism is 'Every human is an animal, every animal is a body, therefore every human is a body'? Actually, that doesn't give the final conclusion. Re-analyze: The final conclusion is 'Every human is a substance'. The premises that directly lead to it are 'Every human is a body' and 'Every body is a substance'. So the main syllogism is: Every human is a body, every body is a substance → every human is a substance. The other premises serve to prove 'Every human is a body'.
4
Distinguish mawsul vs mafsun
Check whether the conclusions of the sub-syllogisms are explicitly stated in the text. In the mawsul example, they are not stated; the chain is continuous. In the mafsun example, each sub-syllogism's conclusion is written out separately.
5
Reconstruct the sub-syllogisms
For the mawsul example: Sub-syllogism 1 (implicit): Every human is an animal, every animal is a body → every human is a body. This intermediate conclusion is not stated but used as a premise in the main syllogism. For the mafsun example: Sub-syllogism 1 (explicit): Every human is an animal, every animal is a body → every human is a body. Sub-syllogism 2: Every animal is a body, every body is a substance → every animal is a substance. Then the main syllogism uses 'every human is a body' (from sub1) and 'every body is a substance' (original) to get the final conclusion. Note: the text's second sub-conclusion is problematic; the corrected version is 'every animal is a substance'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القياس المركب
قالوا : لكن ربما أدرج في القياس قول زائد على مقدمتي القياس، إما غير متعلق بالقياس، أو متعلق به والمتعلق بالقياس إما لترويج الكلام وتحسينه، أو لبيان المقدمتين أو إحداهما، ويسمون هذا القياس المركب.
قالوا: وحاصله يرجع إلى أقيسة متعددة سيقت لبيان مطلوب واحد، إلا أن القياس المبين للمطلوب بالذات منها ليس إلا واحدا والباقي لبيان مقدمات القياس .
قالوا : وربما حذفوا بعض مقدمات القياس، إما تعويلاً على فهم الذهن لها، أو لترويج المغلطة حتى لا يطلع على كذبها عند التصريح بها .
قالوا : ثم إن كانت الأقيسة لبيان المقدمات قد صرح فيها بنتائجها فيسمى القياس «مفصولا»، وإلا فـ «موصول». 
ومثلوا «الموصول» بقول القائل : كل إنسان حيوان ، وكل حيوان جسم، وكل جسم جوهر، فكل إنسان جوهر.
و «المفصول» بقولهم: «كل إنسان حيوان وكل حيوان جسم، فكل إنسان جسم». ثم يقول : كل حيوان جسم، وكل جسم جوهر، فكل إنسان حيوان. فيلزم منهما أن «كل إنسان جوهر».
The compound syllogism
They said: But perhaps an extra statement is inserted into the syllogism beyond its two premises, either unrelated to the syllogism or related to it. The related one is either for embellishing speech and improving it, or for explaining the two premises or one of them. They call this the 'compound syllogism'.
They said: Its summary reduces to multiple syllogisms arranged to explain one desired conclusion, except that the syllogism that explains the desired conclusion by itself is only one of them, and the rest are for explaining the premises of the syllogism.
They said: And perhaps they omit some premises of the syllogism, either relying on the mind's understanding of them, or to promote a fallacy so that its falsehood is not discovered when stated explicitly.
They said: Then if the syllogisms explaining the premises have their results explicitly stated, the syllogism is called 'disconnected' (mafsul), otherwise 'connected' (mawsul).
They gave an example of the 'connected': One says: Every human is an animal, every animal is a body, every body is a substance, so every human is a substance.
And of the 'disconnected': Their saying: 'Every human is an animal and every animal is a body, so every human is a body.' Then he says: Every animal is a body, and every body is a substance, so every human is an animal. So from both it follows that 'every human is a substance'.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage presents the logicians' description of the 'compound syllogism' (al-qiyas al-murakkab). The authors begin by noting that sometimes an extra statement (qawl za'id) is added to a syllogism beyond its two premises. This extra statement may be unrelated or related to the syllogism. If related, it serves either to embellish the speech or to clarify one or both premises. The whole compound syllogism reduces to multiple syllogisms arranged for one desired conclusion (matlub wahid), but only one of them directly proves the conclusion; the others explain the premises of that main syllogism. They also note that sometimes premises are omitted, either because the mind can understand them implicitly or to hide a fallacious premise. Then they distinguish two types: 'connected' (mawsul) where the results of the explanatory syllogisms are not explicitly stated, and 'disconnected' (mafsul) where they are explicitly stated. The examples use categorical syllogisms in the first figure: for mawsul, a chain 'Every human is an animal, every animal is a body, every body is a substance' leading directly to 'Every human is a substance'. For mafsun, they separate the chain into two explicit syllogisms, each with a stated conclusion: first 'Every human is an animal, every animal is a body, so every human is a body', then 'Every animal is a body, every body is a substance, so every human is an animal' (though the second conclusion seems to be a mistake in the text, corrected in footnote to 'so every animal is a substance' perhaps). The two partial results combine to yield the final conclusion. This classification is part of the logical tradition that Ibn Taymiyya will later critique.

Key Terms
القياس المركب
موصول
مفصول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes the 'connected' (mawsul) from the 'disconnected' (mafsul) compound syllogism?
A) The mawsul has premises omitted, while the mafsun states all premises.
B) The mawsul does not explicitly state the conclusions of the sub-syllogisms, while the mafsun does.
C) The mawsul uses only conditional propositions, while the mafsun uses categorical ones.
D) The mawsul is used for demonstration, while the mafsun for dialectic.

Key Takeaway
A compound syllogism consists of multiple syllogisms; the distinction between mawsul and mafsun lies in whether the intermediate conclusions are explicitly stated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Take the following theological argument: 'Every created thing (khalq) has a creator (khaliq). The world (al-'alam) is created. Therefore the world has a creator. But to prove 'the world is created', a sub-syllogism is added: 'Every composite (murakkab) is created; the world is composite; so the world is created.' The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of the Two-Premise Requirement in Compound Syllogism
Ibn Taymiyya critiques the logicians' requirement of only two premises, showing many implicit premises are needed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the hidden premises in a legal syllogism and explain why Ibn Taymiyya argues that more than two premises are often required.

Core Idea
A single claim often depends on a chain of implicit premises; logicians' two-premise rule ignores reality.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges a foundational claim of Aristotelian logic: that a compound syllogism needs only two explicit premises. Using the example of nabidh prohibition, he shows that many background assumptions—like the authenticity of hadith and the binding authority of prophetic commands—must be added, multiplying premises far beyond two. This lesson dissects his argument and its implications for legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim
The logicians assert that any compound syllogism (qiyas murakkab) can be reduced to exactly two premises. Ibn Taymiyya attacks this claim as impractical.
2
Extract the central example
Ibn Taymiyya uses the example of nabidh: 'Nabidh is khamr; every khamr is haram; therefore nabidh is haram.' This looks like a standard two-premise syllogism.
3
List the explicit premises
The explicit premises are: (1) Every khamr is haram. (2) Nabidh is khamr. These alone yield the conclusion.
4
Uncover the implicit premises
Ibn Taymiyya points out that for the argument to be sound, one must also know that the hadith 'Every intoxicant is khamr' is authentic and that whatever the Prophet forbade is forbidden. These implicit premises are not stated but are necessary.
If all such premises were made explicit, the number would exceed two, possibly reaching ten or more.
5
Generalize the critique
Ibn Taymiyya extends his point to other legal arguments, such as the obligation of prayer, zakat, and hajj. In each case, one must invoke the Qur'anic text and the principle that Allah's commands are obligatory, adding more premises.
Rational people consider such repetitive listing as halting speech ( 'لكنة' ) and a waste of time.

Source Text

المقام الثالث - قولهم في «القياس المركب»
وإن شئت قلت: «اتصاف الموصوف بالصفة نفياً أو إثباتاً»؛ وإن شئت قلت: «نسبة المحمول إلى الموضوع والخبر إلى المبتدأ نفياً أو إثباتاً»؛ وأمثال ذلك من العبارات الدالة على المعنى الواحد المقصود بـ «القضية». فإذا كان النتيجة أن النبيذ حرام أو ليس بحرام»، أو «الإنسان حساس أو ليس بحساس»، أو نحو ذلك، فالمطلوب ثبوت التحريم للنبيذ أو انتفاؤه، وكذلك ثبوت الحس للإنسان أو انتفاؤه والمقدمة الواحدة إذا ناسبت ذلك المطلوب حصل بها المقصود. وقولنا: النبيذ خمر يناسب المطلوب، وكذلك قولنا: الإنسان حيوان. فإذا كان الإنسان يعلم أن كل خمر حرام، ولكن يشك في النبيذ المتنازع فيه هل يسمى في لغة الشارع «خمراً»، فقيل: النبيذ حرام لأنه قد ثبت في الصحيح عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال: كل مسكر خمر، كانت هذه القضية، وهي قولنا: «قد قال النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم: إن كل مسكر خمر، يفيد تحريم النبيذ، وإن كان نفس قوله قد تضمن قضية أخرى.
والاستدلال بذلك مشروط بتقديم مقدمات معلومة عند المستمع، وهي إنَّ ما أهلُ العلم بالحديث فقد وجب التصديق بأن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قاله» و «إن ما حرمه الرسول فهو حرام»، ونحو ذلك.
فلو لزم أن يذكر كل ما يتوقف عليه العلم وإن كان معلوماً كانت المقدمات أكثر من اثنتين، بل قد تكون أكثر من عشر. وعلى ما قالوه فينبغي لكل من استدل بقول النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أن يقول: النبي حرم ذلك، وما حرمه فهو حرام، فهذا حرام. وكذلك يقول: النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أوجب، وما أوجبه النبي فقد وجب، فهذا قد وجب.
وإذا احتج على تحريم الأمهات والبنات ونحو ذلك يحتاج أن يقول: إن الله حرم هذا في القرآن وما حرمه الله فهو حرام. وإذا احتج على وجوب الصلاة، والزكاة، والحج بمثل قول الله تعالى: ﴿ وَلِلَّهِ عَلَى النَّاسِ حِجُ الْبَيْتِ ﴾، يقول: إن الله أوجب الحج في كتابه، وما أوجبه الله فهو واجب. وأمثال ذلك مما يعده العقلاء لكنة، وعياً، وإيضاحاً للواضح.
Third station - Their statement about 'compound syllogism'
And if you wish, say: 'The attribution of a quality to the qualified, negatively or affirmatively'; and if you wish, say: 'The relation of the predicate to the subject and the predicate to the subject of a nominal sentence, negatively or affirmatively'; and the like of such expressions indicating the one meaning intended by 'proposition'. So if the conclusion is that nabidh is forbidden or not forbidden, or that man is sentient or not sentient, or something similar, then the required matter is the establishment of prohibition for nabidh or its negation, and similarly the establishment of sentience for man or its negation. And a single premise, if it suits that required matter, achieves the intended goal. And our statement: 'Nabidh is khamr' suits the required matter, and likewise our statement: 'Man is an animal'. So if a person knows that every khamr is forbidden, but doubts concerning the disputed nabidh whether it is called 'khamr' in the language of the Lawgiver, and it is said: 'Nabidh is forbidden because it has been established in the authentic hadith from the Prophet, peace be upon him, that he said: Every intoxicant is khamr', then this proposition, which is our statement: 'The Prophet, peace be upon him, said: Every intoxicant is khamr', benefits the prohibition of nabidh, even if his statement itself contains another proposition.
And reasoning by that is conditional upon presenting premises known to the listener, which are: that 'the people of hadith knowledge have verified the truth that the Prophet, peace be upon him, said it' and 'what the Messenger forbade is forbidden', and the like.
So if it were necessary to mention everything on which knowledge depends, even if it is known, then the premises would be more than two, indeed they could be more than ten. And according to what they said, it would be appropriate for everyone who reasons by the statement of the Prophet, peace be upon him, to say: 'The Prophet forbade that, and what he forbade is forbidden, so this is forbidden.' And similarly he says: 'The Prophet, peace be upon him, obligated, and what the Prophet obligated is obligatory, so this is obligatory.'
And if he argues for the prohibition of mothers and daughters and the like, he needs to say: 'Allah forbade this in the Qur'an, and what Allah forbade is forbidden.' And if he argues for the obligation of prayer, zakat, and hajj by the like of Allah's statement: 'And to Allah is the duty upon people to make pilgrimage to the House', he says: 'Allah obligated hajj in His Book, and what Allah obligated is obligatory.' And the like of that which the rational people consider as halting speech, defect, and clarification of the obvious.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation by Ibn Taymiyya of the logicians' claim that a compound syllogism (القياس المركب) requires only two premises. He begins by defining what a proposition (القضية) is: an assertion of a predicate to a subject, either affirmatively or negatively. Then he gives an example: if the conclusion is that nabidh (a fermented drink) is forbidden, the required matter is to establish the prohibition. A single premise like 'Nabidh is khamr' is suitable, but only if one already knows that every khamr is forbidden. However, in practice, further premises are needed, such as the authenticity of the hadith 'Every intoxicant is khamr' and the principle that whatever the Prophet forbade is forbidden. Ibn Taymiyya argues that if one had to explicitly state all such background knowledge, the number of premises would multiply far beyond two. He illustrates with examples from Islamic law: when arguing for the prohibition of mothers or the obligation of hajj, one must mention the Qur'anic text and the general principle that what Allah commands is obligatory. Rational people consider such endless repetition as halting speech (لكنة) and a waste of time. Thus, the logicians' insistence on only two premises is impractical and ignores the many implicit premises required for any real argument.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya in this passage, why does the argument that nabidh is haram require more than two premises?
A) Because the hadith must be authenticated, and the principle that the Prophet's prohibitions are binding must be added.
B) Because the logicians have proven that the conclusion is false.
C) Because the definition of nabidh is unclear.
D) Because all legal arguments require exactly ten premises.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya demonstrates that practical reasoning, especially in law, always involves many hidden premises, undermining the logicians' neat two-premise model.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the argument: 'Every intoxicant is khamr; nabidh is an intoxicant; therefore nabidh is haram.' List at least three implicit premises that Ibn Taymiyya would argue are needed for this argument to be complete. Then explain how this supports his critique of the two-premise requirement.
?
Consider the argument for the obligation of hajj from Qur'anic verse. Identify the explicit premises and at least two implicit premises that would be needed, following Ibn Taymiyya's reasoning.
